Permissibility of Rabbit
Confirms the permissibility of rabbit with hadith and qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that rabbit is halal in the Hanafi school and explain the proof from hadith and analogy.

Core Idea
Rabbit is halal by explicit prophetic command and because it is not a predator nor a carrion-eater, resembling deer.

This lesson covers the final part of the passage, which explicitly permits rabbit. The proof is a hadith where the Prophet ordered his companions to eat roasted rabbit, and an analogy to deer. This resolves any doubt about rabbit being similar to predators.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on rabbit
The text states 'wa hill al-arnab' (and the rabbit is lawful). This is a clear ruling of permissibility.
This is the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.
2
Locate the primary proof: hadith
The Prophet ordered his companions to eat rabbit when it was presented to him roasted. The hadith is narrated by Ahmad and al-Nasa'i.
The command indicates permissibility (ibahah).
3
Understand the secondary proof: analogy (qiyas)
Rabbit resembles deer (zaby) because it is not a predator (sabu') and does not eat carrion. Deer is halal by consensus, so rabbit is analogized to it.
This counters any argument that rabbit is haram due to having fangs or claws.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Fish and Locusts: The Exception from Slaughter Requirements
Statutes that locusts, like fish, are halal without ritual slaughter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that fish and locusts are halal without ritual slaughter and explain the hadith basis for this exception.

Core Idea
Fish and locusts are halal without slaughter – a special dispensation from dhabh.

In Hanafi fiqh, ritual slaughter (dhabh) is generally required for the consumption of land animals and birds. However, two types of creatures are exempt from this requirement: fish and locusts. This lesson examines the concise ruling recorded in the commentary of Kanz al-Daqaiq, which affirms that these are lawful without slaughter, supported by a prophetic narration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The passage states that fish and locusts are halal without slaughter. Note the phrase 'وحل بلا ذكاة كالجراد' – 'and it is lawful without slaughter like locusts.'
The ruling applies to both fish and locusts equally.
2
Locate the Proof
The mention of 'لما روينا' refers to a hadith: 'Two dead things are lawful for us: fish and locusts.' This is the primary evidence for the exception.
The hadith is recorded in Sunan Ibn Majah and Musnad Ahmad.
3
Note the Limitation
This exception is exclusive to fish and locusts. All other animals require proper dhabh for consumption.
This is a limited dispensation, not a general rule for all sea or insect life.

Source Text

And it is lawful without slaughter like locusts, meaning fish is lawful without slaughter like locusts, due to what we have narrated.

This is a concise ruling statement that fish and locusts are permissible without requiring ritual slaughter (dhabh). The commentator clarifies that fish is halal without slaughter, just like locusts, based on narrations. This is a well-known Hanafi ruling supported by hadith (the Prophet said, 'Two dead things are lawful for us: fish and locusts').

Key Terms
ذكاة (dhakāh): Ritual slaughter. In this context, it is not required for fish and locusts.
الجراد (al-jarād): Locusts, a type of insect that is halal to eat without slaughter.

Signs of Life in Slaughtered Animals
Ruling with proof on signs of life after slaughter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two primary signs of life after slaughter and apply the Hanafi criteria for determining whether an animal was alive at the time of slaughter.

Core Idea
Movement or blood exit after slaughter proves life; absence makes it carrion.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq explains how to determine if an animal was alive when slaughtered. The general rule is simple: if the animal moves or blood exits after the throat is cut, it is halal. If neither occurs, it is haram. However, the text also addresses edge cases and intra-madhhab differences, which we will unpack step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Find the statement that movement or blood exit makes the meat halal. Note the rationale: only a living being can move or bleed. Understand that absence of both signs indicates death before slaughter.
2
Analyze the exception by Muhammad ibn Muqatil
Muhammad ibn Muqatil holds that blood exit without movement is not sufficient because blood may exit after death. This shows that movement is the more reliable sign.
This is a minority view; the majority consider either sign sufficient.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on degree of life
Three opinions: Abu Hanifa requires that the animal would have lived a day without slaughter; Abu Yusuf that similar animals would live; Muhammad that it lives longer than a normal slaughtered animal. Understand the practical difference.
The mufta bihi is the Sarajiyya case: if only the life of a slaughtered animal remains, it is halal.
4
Apply the detailed signs from Muhammad ibn Salama
Muhammad ibn Salama lists specific signs: closing mouth/eye/drawing leg/standing hair = life; opening mouth/eye/extending leg/lying hair = death. Practice mapping each sign to the ruling.
These signs are based on relaxation (death) vs contraction (life).
5
Consider the wolf attack case and fall from roof
The text concludes with two practical cases: a wolf-teared belly and a sheep falling from a roof after slaughter. Both are halal because slaughter was validly performed on a living animal.
The fatwa is on the wolf case: if only the life of a slaughtered animal remains, it is halal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ذبح شاة فتحركت أو خرج الدم حلت والألم يدر حياته) لأن الحياة أو خروج الدم لا يكونان إلا من الحي لأن الميت لا يتحرك ولا يخرج منه الدم فيكون وجودهما أو وجود أحدهما دليل الحياة فيحل، وعدمهما علامة الموت فلا يحل. وذكر محمد بن مقاتل : إن خرج الدم ولم تتحرك لا تحل لأن الدم لا يجمد عند الموت فيجوز بخروج الدم وهذا سيأتي في المنخنقة والمتردية والنطيحة والتي بقر الذئب بطنها لأن ذكاة هذه الأشياء تحلل وإن كانت حياته خفية في ظاهر الرواية لقوله تعالى إلا ما ذكيتم﴾ [المائدة : ٣] وعن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى أنها تحل إذا كانت بحال تعيش يوماً لولا الذكاة. وعن الثاني : إن كان لا يعيش مثلها لا تحل وعن محمد: إن كانت بحال يعيش فوق ما يعيش المذبوح حل وإلا فلا. ولو ذبح شاة مريضة ولم يتخرك منها إلا فرهها قال محمد بن سلمة: إن فتحت فاها لا تؤكل وإن ضمته تؤكل، وإن فتحت عينها لا تؤكل، وإن ضمت عينها أكلت وإن مدت رجلها لا تؤكل وإن ضمتها تؤكل، وإن قام شعرها تؤكل وإن نام لا لأن الحيوان يسترخي بالموت ففتح الفم والعين ومد الرجل ونوم الشعر تؤكل، وهذا صحيح علامة الموت لأنها استرخاء، وضم الفم وتغميض العين وقبض الرجل وقيام الشعر ليس باسترخاء بل حركات تختص بالحي فتدل على الحياة وفي السراجية : إذا شق الذئب بطن الشاة ولم يبق فيها من الحياة إلا بقدر ما يبقى في المذبوح بعد الذبح فذبحت حلت وعليه الفتوى ا هـ . ولو ذبحت شاة على سطح فوقعت فماتت تحل لأنها صارت مذكاة بقطع محل
And if he slaughters a sheep and it moves or blood comes out, it is lawful, because life senses its life? Because life or the exit of blood only occurs from the living, because the dead does not move and blood does not come out of it. So their existence or existence of one of them is evidence of life, so it is lawful. And their absence is a sign of death, so it is not lawful. And Muhammad ibn Muqatil mentioned: If blood comes out and it does not move, it is not lawful, because blood does not coagulate at death, so it is possible due to the exit of blood. And this will come in al-Munkhaniqah, al-Mutaraddiyah, al-Natihah, and that which the wolf tore its belly, because the slaughter of these things makes them lawful even if their life is hidden in the apparent narration due to the verse 'except what you slaughter' [al-Ma'ida:3]. And from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, that it is lawful if it is in a state that it would live a day without slaughter. And from the second (Abu Yusuf): If one like it does not live, it is not lawful. And from Muhammad: If it is in a state that it lives beyond what a slaughtered animal lives, it is lawful, otherwise not. And if he slaughters a sick sheep and no movement occurs from it except its...? Muhammad ibn Salama said: If it opens its mouth, it is not eaten; if it closes it, it is eaten. If it opens its eye, it is not eaten; if it closes its eye, it is eaten. If it extends its leg, it is not eaten; if it draws it in, it is eaten. If its hair stands, it is eaten; if it lies down, it is not. Because the animal relaxes at death, so opening the mouth and eye, extending the leg, and lying down of hair are signs of death because they are relaxation. Closing the mouth, closing the eye, drawing in the leg, and standing of hair are not relaxation but movements specific to the living, so they indicate life. And in al-Sirajiyyah: If a wolf tears the belly of a sheep and no life remains in it except the amount that remains in a slaughtered animal after slaughter, and he slaughters it, it is lawful, and upon that is the fatwa. End. And if he slaughters a sheep on a roof and it falls and dies, it is lawful because it becomes slaughtered by cutting the place.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling with proofs on the conditions for a slaughtered animal being considered alive at the time of slaughter. The general principle: if the animal moves or blood exits after the throat is cut, it is evidence of life, so the meat is halal. Absence of both indicates death before slaughter, making it carrion. Muhammad ibn Muqatil adds that if blood exits but no movement, it is not sufficient because blood may exit after death. The verse 'except what you slaughter' (5:3) permits animals that were rendered unconscious but have hidden life. Abu Hanifa said it is halal if the animal would have lived a day without slaughter; Abu Yusuf said if similar animals do not live (meaning if it was near death), it is not halal; Muhammad al-Shaybani said if it lives longer than a normal slaughtered animal (after the wound), it is halal. Muhammad ibn Salama gives detailed signs: opening mouth/eye and extending leg are signs of death (relaxation), while closing them or drawing in are signs of life. Hair standing is life, lying down is death. The Sarajiyya mentions the case of a wolf tearing the belly, where if the animal has only the life of a slaughtered animal, it is halal by slaughter. Finally, if a sheep is slaughtered on a roof and falls to its death, it is halal because the slaughter was valid.

Key Terms
المنخنقة (al-munkhaniqah): An animal that has been strangled to death; here mentioned as a case where hidden life may still be present.
المتردية (al-mutaraddiyah): An animal that has fallen from a height and died.
النطيحة (al-natīḥah): An animal that has been gored to death by another animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The animal opens its mouth.
B) The animal closes its eye.
C) The animal extends its leg.
D) The animal's hair lies down.

Key Takeaway
The primary signs of life after slaughter are movement or blood exit; in their absence, the animal is considered carrion.

Fish and Locusts: Permissible Without Slaughter (Hanafi Ruling)
Ruling with proof on fish and locusts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fish and locusts without slaughter, identify the proof from hadith, and distinguish the ruling on floating fish.

Core Idea
Fish and locusts are halal without slaughter because the Prophet exempted them; floating fish is makruh, not haram.

The Hanafi school affirms that fish and locusts do not require ritual slaughter (dhabh) to be permissible. This ruling is based on specific hadith evidence. However, floating fish (al-tafi) is considered makruh tanzihi due to another prophetic tradition. In this lesson, we examine the proofs and the conditions for a fish to be halal when its cause of death is unknown.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The text first states that non-floating fish and locusts are halal without slaughter. Note that the ruling applies to these two specific animals; all other animals require slaughter.
The exception is established by prophetic permission.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The hadith 'Two dead things are made lawful for us: fish and locusts' is the core proof. Additionally, the hadith from Jabir regarding floating fish is cited.
Both are ahad hadith, so their thubut is zanni.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'halal' in the hadith indicates permissibility. For floating fish, the Prophet's command 'do not eat it' indicates karahah (dislike), not tahrim (prohibition), because the default in food is halal unless proven haram.
Hanafi usul: a prohibition without evidence of tahrim indicates karahah tanzihiyyah.
4
Understand the condition for dead fish found
If the cause of death is known (e.g., thrown by sea, trapped, killed by bird or cold), it is halal. If the cause is unknown (e.g., died of disease), it is not eaten.
The principle is that certainty of the cause removes the default prohibition of maytah.
5
Note the inter-madhhab dispute
Imam Malik permits floating fish based on general permissibility. The Hanafi school restricts it using Jabir's hadith, making it makruh.
This is a difference in the interpretation of the same hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الذبائح
السمك غير طاف وحل بلا ذكاة كالجراد ولو ذبح شاة فتحركت أو خرج الدم حلت
من حر إلى خبيث. ونهى عليه الصلاة والسلام عن دواء اتخذ فيه الضفدع ونهى عن بيع السرطان. والميتة المذكورة فيما تلي محمولة على حالة الاضطرار وهو مباح فيما لا يحل أكله والميتة والمذكاة فيهما سواء، وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام أحل لنا ميتتان السمك والجراد ودمان الكبد والطحال لا دليل لهم في هذا الحديث لأن بالميتة ما ألقاه البحر حتى يكون موته مضافاً البحر ولا يتناول ما مات فيه بمرض أو نحوه. وأما الطافي فيكره أكله لقوله جابر أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال ما نضب عنه الماء فكلوا وما طفى فلا تأكلوه وهو حجة على مالك في إباحة الطافي فالأصل في هذا ما عرف سبب موته كلفظ البحر أو يحبسه في مكان كالحظيرة الصغيرة بحيث يمكن أخذه من غير حيلة أو ابتلاع سمكة أو بقتل طير الماء إياها أو إجماد الماء عليها حل أكلها لأن سبب موتها معلوم. ولو ماتت برده أو انحسر الماء الماء أو شدة عن بعضه ومات روى هشام عن محمد إن كان رأسه على الماء لا يؤكل، وإن كان ذنبه في الماء ورأسه انحسر عنه الماء أكل لأن خروج رأسه من الماء سبب لموته فكان معلوماً بخلاف خروج ذنبه، فحاصله أن الشرط فيه أن يعلم سبب موته حتى لو أبان عضواً يضره فإنه يؤكل ويؤكل العضو أيضاً. قال رحمه الله : وحل بلا ذكاة كالجراد يعني يحل السمك بلا ذكاة كالجراد لما روينا.
Book of Slaughtered Animals. Fish that is not floating is permissible without slaughter, like locusts. And if one slaughters a sheep and it moves or blood flows out, it becomes permissible from [؟] to evil. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade a remedy containing a frog and forbade selling crabs. And the dead animal mentioned above is construed as a case of necessity, and it is permissible for what is not permissible to eat; the dead and the slaughtered are the same in it. And his saying (peace be upon him): 'Two dead things are made lawful for us: fish and locusts, and two bloods: the liver and the spleen.' This hadith is no proof for them because 'dead' refers to what the sea casts out so that its death is attributed to the sea, and it does not include what dies in it from disease or the like. As for the floating [fish], it is disliked to eat because of Jabir's report that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'What the water recedes from, eat it, and what floats, do not eat it.' This is an argument against Malik who permits the floating. The principle in this is that if the cause of its death is known—such as being cast out by the sea, or being trapped in a place like a small enclosure such that it can be taken without contrivance, or swallowing a fish, or a water bird killing it, or ice forming over it—then eating it is permissible because the cause of its death is known. If it dies from cold, or the water recedes from some of it, and it dies, Hisham narrated from Muhammad: if its head is on the water, it is not eaten; if its tail is in the water and its head is exposed from the water, it is eaten because the emergence of its head from the water is a cause of its death, so it is known, unlike the emergence of its tail. The upshot is that the condition is that the cause of its death is known, so if it loses a limb that harms it, then it is eaten and the limb is also eaten. He (author) said: 'And it is permissible without slaughter like locusts,' meaning fish is permissible without slaughter like locusts because of what we narrated.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Book of Slaughtered Animals. It begins by stating that non-floating fish is halal without ritual slaughter (dhabh), similar to locusts. The text then addresses the case of slaughtering a sheep where movement or blood flow indicates life, making the meat halal. It mentions prohibitions against using frogs in medicine and selling crabs. The discussion shifts to dead animals (maytah): the dead animal mentioned earlier refers to a situation of necessity, and it is permissible only for what is otherwise impermissible to eat; both dead and slaughtered animals are considered the same in that context. The hadith 'Two dead things are made lawful for us: fish and locusts, and two bloods: the liver and the spleen' is cited, but the author argues that this hadith does not support the opponent's view because 'dead' here means what the sea casts out, not what dies from disease. Then the text discusses floating fish (al-tafi): it is makruh (disliked) based on a hadith from Jabir. This is an argument against Imam Malik who permits it. The general principle is that if the cause of death is known—such as being thrown by the sea, trapped, eaten by another fish, killed by a bird, or frozen in ice—the fish is halal. If it dies from cold or receding water, there are details: if the head is above water, it is not eaten; if the tail is in water and head exposed, it is eaten because the head emerging is a known cause of death. The key condition is knowledge of the cause of death; if a harmful limb is removed, both the fish and the limb are eaten. The commentator then repeats the matn: 'It is permissible without slaughter like locusts,' and explains that this means fish is permissible without slaughter, like locusts, based on the transmitted proof.

Key Terms
ذبائح (dhaba'ih): Refers to the chapter on slaughtered animals, covering rulings on meat.
طافي (tafi): Fish that floats on the water's surface after dying.
ذكاة (dhabh): Ritual slaughter according to Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Makruh (disliked)
C) Haram (forbidden)
D) Mubah (permissible but floating is better avoided)

Key Takeaway
Fish and locusts are halal without slaughter due to prophetic exemption; floating fish is disliked (makruh), not prohibited.

Signs of Life at Slaughter: Movement, Blood Flow, and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Details the conditions that indicate the animal was alive at time of slaughter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi signs indicating life at slaughter and distinguish the ruling when only blood flows versus no movement, applying the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
Movement or blood flow signals life; both absent means haram, but knowledge of life overrides all signs.

The Hanafi school provides detailed criteria to determine if an animal was alive at the moment of slaughter, affecting the halal status of the meat. While movement and blood flow are primary indicators, scholars differ on whether blood alone suffices. This lesson unpacks these differences and clarifies the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The passage begins with a clear rule: if a slaughtered sheep moves or blood flows, it is halal. Absence of both makes it haram. Recognize that this establishes a default presumption.
2
Note Muhammad ibn Muqatil's dissent
Muhammad ibn Muqatil disagrees: blood flow without movement does not confirm life, because blood may not coagulate immediately after death. This challenges the base rule and introduces intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
His argument will be revisited in the context of strangled animals.
3
Examine the three positions of the founding Imams
Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani each give a different benchmark for when a nearly-dead animal is considered alive enough for slaughter. Study each criterion: living a day (Abu Hanifa), living as long as its like (Abu Yusuf), living longer than a slaughtered animal (Muhammad).
The mufta bihi is generally Abu Hanifa's position as upheld by later authorities.
4
Analyze the practical signs from Muhammad ibn Salamah
For a sick sheep, specific bodily indicators distinguish life from death. Active closures (mouth, eye, leg) indicate life; passive openings indicate death due to relaxation. Memorize this list as a diagnostic tool.
This is a practical application of the principle that relaxation (istirkha') signals death.
5
Understand the wolf-attack exception and final rule
If a wolf attacks and the animal has trace life (like a freshly slaughtered one), slaughter makes it halal. Moreover, if life is known with certainty, no visible signs are needed. This is the definitive position in al-Muhit.
Consensus applies when life is stable; otherwise the dispute continues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ولو ذبح شاة فتحركت أو خرج الدم حلت والألم يدر حياته لأن الحياة أو خروج الدم لا يكونان إلا من الحي لأن الميت لا يتحرك ولا يخرج منه الدم فيكون وجودهما أو وجود أحدهما دليل الحياة فيحل، وعدمهما علامة الموت فلا يحل. وذكر محمد بن مقاتل : إن خرج الدم ولم تتحرك لا تحل لأن الدم لا يجمد عند الموت فيجوز بخروج الدم وهذا سيأتي في المنخنقة والمتردية والنطيحة والتي بقر الذئب بطنها لأن ذكاة هذه الأشياء تحلل وإن كانت حياته خفية في ظاهر الرواية لقوله تعالى ﴿إلا ما ذكيتم﴾ [المائدة : ٣] وعن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى أنها تحل إذا كانت بحال تعيش يوماً لولا الذكاة. وعن الثاني : إن كان لا يعيش مثلها لا تحل وعن محمد: إن كانت بحال يعيش فوق ما يعيش المذبوح حل وإلا فلا. ولو ذبح شاة مريضة ولم يتخرك منها إلا فرهها قال محمد بن سلمة: إن فتحت فاها لا تؤكل وإن ضمته تؤكل، وإن فتحت عينها لا تؤكل، وإن ضمت عينها أكلت وإن مدت رجلها لا تؤكل وإن ضمتها تؤكل، وإن قام شعرها تؤكل وإن نام لا لأن الحيوان يسترخي بالموت ففتح الفم والعين ومد الرجل ونوم الشعر تؤكل، وهذا صحيح علامة الموت لأنها استرخاء، وضم الفم وتغميض العين وقبض الرجل وقيام الشعر ليس باسترخاء بل حركات تختص بالحي فتدل على الحياة وفي السراجية : إذا شق الذئب بطن الشاة ولم يبق فيها من الحياة إلا بقدر ما يبقى في المذبوح بعد الذبح فذبحت حلت وعليه الفتوى ا هـ . ولو ذبحت شاة على سطح فوقعت فماتت تحل لأنها صارت مذكاة بقطع محل الذكاة؛ كذا في المحيط. وفيه أيضاً : إذا شق الذئب بطن الشاة إن كان فيها حياة مستقرة حلت بالإجماع وإلا لا، سواء عاش أو لم يعش عند الإمام وهو الصحيح وعليه الفتوى ا هـ . رحمه الله : (وإن علم حياته وإن لم تتحرك ولم يخرج الدم يعني إذا علم حياة الشاة وقت الذبح حلت بالذكاة تحركت أو لا، خرج منها دم أو لا ؛ كذا في المحيط والله تعالى أعلم بالصواب.
He (the author) said: If one slaughters a sheep and it moves or blood comes out, it becomes halal, and pain does [not] preclude its life. Because life or blood flow only come from a living being; the dead does not move nor does blood come out of it. So their existence or the existence of one of them is evidence of life, so it is halal; their absence is a sign of death, so it is not halal. And Muhammad ibn Muqatil mentioned: If blood comes out but it does not move, it is not halal because blood does not coagulate at death, so it is possible that the blood came out? This will come in the [discussion of] the strangled, beaten, gored, and that whose belly is torn by a wolf. Because the slaughter of these things makes them halal even if their life is hidden, according to the apparent narration, due to His saying: 'except what you slaughter' (Quran 5:3). And from Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) that it becomes halal if it is in a state where it would live for a day without slaughter. From the Second (Abu Yusuf): If one like it would not live, it is not halal. From Muhammad: If it is in a state where it would live longer than a slaughtered animal lives, it is halal; otherwise not. If one slaughters a sick sheep and only [a part?] does not move? Muhammad ibn Salamah said: If it opens its mouth, it is not eaten; if it closes it, it is eaten. If it opens its eye, it is not eaten; if it closes its eye, it is eaten. If it extends its leg, it is not eaten; if it draws it, it is eaten. If its hair stands on end, it is eaten; if it lies down, it is not. Because the animal relaxes at death: opening the mouth, eye, extending the leg, and lying down of hair indicate death because of relaxation. Closing the mouth, closing the eye, drawing the leg, and raising of hair are not relaxation but movements specific to the living, so they indicate life. In al-Sirajiyyah: If a wolf splits the belly of a sheep and there remains in it only as much life as remains in a slaughtered animal after slaughter, and it is slaughtered, it is halal; and upon that is the fatwa. End. If a sheep is slaughtered on a roof and falls and dies, it is halal because it became slaughtered by cutting the place of slaughter; as in al-Muhit. In it also: If a wolf splits the belly of a sheep, if it has stable life, it is halal by consensus; otherwise no, whether it lives or not according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa), and that is correct, and upon that is the fatwa. End. He (the author) said: 'And if his life is known even if it does not move and blood does not come out' means if the life of the sheep at the time of slaughter is known, it is halal by slaughter, whether it moves or not, whether blood comes out of it or not; as in al-Muhit. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the signs that establish the animal was alive at the time of slaughter, making the meat halal. The base ruling states that if a slaughtered sheep moves or blood flows, it is halal because these are exclusive to the living. The absence of both indicates death. Muhammad ibn Muqatil dissents: if blood flows but no movement, it is not halal, arguing that blood does not coagulate immediately at death, so blood flow could occur post-mortem. The commentator notes this will be revisited in the discussion of animals that die from strangulation, beating, etc. The primary evidence is Quran 5:3 'except what you slaughter,' which allows slaughter even when life is hidden. Three positions are then presented: (1) Abu Hanifa: halal if the animal would have lived a day without slaughter. (2) Abu Yusuf: halal only if an animal in that condition would normally survive. (3) Muhammad: halal if it would live longer than a normally slaughtered animal lives; otherwise not. Next, details for a sick sheep: Muhammad ibn Salamah lists signs: open mouth/eye and extended leg indicate death (since the body relaxes at death); closed mouth/eye, drawn leg, and raised hair indicate life (these are active movements). Then from al-Sirajiyyah: if a wolf attacks a sheep and only a trace of life remains (like that of a freshly slaughtered animal), then slaughter makes it halal. This is the relied-upon position. Similarly, if a sheep is slaughtered on a roof and falls and dies, it is halal because the cut was made at the proper place. Finally, if the animal's life is known for certain at the time of slaughter, it is halal even without visible movement or blood flow.

Key Terms
حياة (hayat): Life; the state of being alive at the time of slaughter, necessary for the meat to be halal.
حركة (harakah): Movement of the animal after slaughter, considered a clear sign of life.
استرخاء (istirkha'): Relaxation of the body at death, indicating the animal was already dead before slaughter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a sheep is slaughtered and blood flows but there is no movement?
A) It is halal because blood flow alone is sufficient proof of life.
B) It is not halal according to Muhammad ibn Muqatil, but the relied-upon position is that it is halal if life is known otherwise.
C) It is haram because movement is necessary.
D) It is makruh but permissible.

Key Takeaway
Movement or blood flow indicates life at slaughter; if both are absent, the meat is haram unless life is otherwise certain.

Slaughter Validity with Known Life but No Visible Signs
Ruling on slaughter validity based on life knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of life for valid slaughter and apply the ruling to cases where life is known but no movement or blood is observed.

Core Idea
Knowledge of life at slaughter validates even without movement or blood—certainty outweighs visible signs.

Many assume that a slaughtered animal must move or bleed to be halal. The Hanafi school clarifies that what matters is certain knowledge of life at the moment of slaughter. This ruling, drawn from al-Muhit, removes doubt in practical situations where signs of life are not visible. The wolf attack case further establishes that stable life is the key criterion, and the relied-upon position is that of Imam Abu Hanifa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The text states that if the animal's life is known at slaughter, the meat is halal even if the animal does not move or bleed. Note that this is an expansion of the general rule: the condition is knowledge of life, not visible signs.
The ruling is straightforward but often misunderstood.
2
Understand the key term: stable life
The wolf case introduces 'stable life' (hayaah mustaqirrah). This means the animal was alive with the capacity to survive at the time of the attack. If stable life existed, the meat is halal by consensus; otherwise not, regardless of later survival.
Distinguish from mere signs of life like movement or bleeding.
3
Apply to similar cases
Consider scenarios like stunned animals, shock, or severe injury before slaughter. As long as you are certain of life at the moment of cutting, the slaughter is valid. The absence of movement or blood is irrelevant.
Certainty must be based on sound evidence (e.g., seeing the animal alive seconds before).
4
Note the authority
The passage cites al-Muhit and states that the view of Imam Abu Hanifa is the correct one and the basis for fatwa. This helps you prioritize rulings in practice.
The consensus on the wolf case reinforces the stability of the principle.
5
Extract the legal maxim
The underlying maxim: 'Al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk' (Certainty is not removed by doubt). Knowledge of life is certain; lack of movement is not evidence of death.
This maxim applies broadly in fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والألم يدر حياته وإن علم حياته وإن لم تتحرك ولم يخرج الدم. الذكاة؛ كذا في المحيط. وفيه أيضاً : إذا شق الذئب بطن الشاة إن كان فيها حياة مستقرة حلت بالإجماع وإلا لا، سواء عاش أو لم يعش عند الإمام وهو الصحيح وعليه الفتوى ا هـ . رحمه الله : (وإن علم حياته وإن لم تتحرك ولم يخرج الدم يعني إذا علم حياة الشاة وقت الذبح حلت بالذكاة تحركت أو لا، خرج منها دم أو لا ؛ كذا في المحيط والله تعالى أعلم قال بالصواب .
The pain indicates its life, and even if its life is known although it did not move and blood did not come out. The slaughter (is valid) as in al-Muhit. And in it also: If a wolf tears the belly of a sheep, if there was stable life in it, it is permissible by consensus, otherwise not, whether it lived or not according to Imam, and that is the correct view and the fatwa is upon it. May Allah have mercy on him: 'And if its life is known although it did not move and blood did not come out' means if the life of the sheep is known at the time of slaughter, it is permissible by slaughter whether it moved or not, whether blood came out of it or not, as in al-Muhit. And Allah knows best. He said correctly.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage states two rulings from al-Muhit regarding slaughter. First, if the animal's life is clearly known at the time of slaughter—even if it does not move or blood does not gush out—the slaughter is valid and the meat is halal. The key is the certainty of life, not the visible signs. Second, if a wolf attacks a sheep and tears its belly, the ruling depends on whether the sheep had stable life at that moment. If it did, the meat is halal by consensus; if not, it is not halal. According to Imam Abu Hanifa (al-Imam), this applies whether the sheep later lived or died, and this is the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Key Terms
الذكاة (al-dhakaah): Slaughter according to shariah, which makes the animal permissible to eat
حياة مستقرة (hayaah mustaqirrah): Stable life, meaning the animal is alive with the capacity to survive

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because knowledge of life is sufficient.
B) No, because movement and blood are required.
C) Only if it moves slightly.
D) Only if blood comes out.

Key Takeaway
For valid slaughter, certainty of life at the moment of cutting suffices—visible signs like movement or blood are not required.

Milk of She-Ass and Horse: Following the Ruling of Meat
Ruling on milk of she-ass and horse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the milk of a she-ass and horse, and explain the intra-madhhab difference on horse milk.

Core Idea
Milk takes the ruling of the meat it comes from.

The Hanafi school applies the principle that milk is generated from meat, so the ruling on milk follows the ruling on the animal's meat. This lesson examines the case of she-ass milk (clearly disliked) and horse milk (disputed within the school).

Guided Path
1
Identify the animals
Name the two animals: she-ass (female donkey) and horse. Note that donkey meat is haram, while horse meat is disputed.
2
Apply the qiyas rule
Understand that milk is considered to originate from meat, so the ruling (halal/haram/makruh) transfers from meat to milk.
This is an application of qiyas in fiqh.
3
State the ruling for she-ass milk
She-ass milk is makruh (disliked) because she-ass meat is haram. The link is direct: meat is haram, milk follows.
The text says 'kariha' – it is disliked, not haram.
4
State the ruling for horse milk
Imam Abu Hanifa rules horse milk makruh because he considers horse meat makruh. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) do not consider horse meat makruh, so they would not dislike the milk.
This intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is recorded in Fatawa Qadikhan.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The later relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that horse meat is halal (Sahibayn’s view), so horse milk is halal. However, caution is observed in some circles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأكل والشرب
قدم فصل الأكل والشرب على غيره لأن الاحتياج إلى بيان مسائله أهم من غيره. قال رحمه الله : (كره لبن (الأتان لأن اللبن يتولد من اللحم فصار مثله، وكذا لبن الخيل يكره عند الإمام كلحمه عنده، واختلف في كراهة لحم الخيل عندهما ؛ كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان. ولا تؤكل الجلالة ولا يشرب لبنها لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام نهى عن أكلها وشرب لبنها. التي تعتاد أكل الجيف ولا تخلط فيكون لحمها منتناً، ولو حبست حتى يزول والجلالة هي النتن حلت ولم يقدر لذلك مدة في الأصل، وقدر في النوادر بشهر، وقيل بأربعين يوماً في الإبل، وبعشرين يوماً في البقر، وبعشرة أيام في الشاة، وثلاثة أيام في الدجاجة. والتي تخلط بأن تتناول النجاسة والجيف وتتناول غيرها على وجه لا يظهر أثر ذلك في لحمها فلا بأس بحلها ولهذا يحل أكل جذع تغذى بلبن الخنزير لأن لحمه لا يتغير وما تغدى به يصير مستهلكاً لا يبقى له أثر ولهذا قالوا : لا بأس بأكل الدجاج لأنها تخلط ولا يتغير لحمه، وما روي أن الدجاج يحبس ثلاثة أيام ثم يذبح ذلك على وجه القربة لا على أنه شرط. وفي المحيط : ولا بأس بأكل شعير يوجد في بعر الإبل والشاة فيغسل ويؤكل، وإن في أحشاء البقر وروث الفرس لا يؤكل لأن البعر صلب فلا تتداخل النجاسة في أجزاء الشعير والحنطة. ولا بأس بأكل دود الزيتون قبل أن تنفخ فيه الروح لأن إثم اميت إنما يطلق على من له روح. ويكره دفع الجهد من السقاية وحمله إلى المنزل لأنه وضع للشرب لا للحمل، والحطب الذي يوجد في الماء إن كان لا قيمة له فهو حلال لأنه مأذون في أخذه، وإن كان له قيمة فلا . ولا بأس بمضغ العلك للنساء لأن سنهن أضعف من سن الرجال فأقيم العلك لهن مقام السواك، ولا بأس للنساء بخضاب اليد والرجل ما لم يكن خضاب فيه تماثيل، ويكره للرجال والصبيان لأن ذلك تزين وهو مباح للنساء دون الرجال. ولا بأس بخضاب
Chapter on Eating and Drinking. He placed the chapter on eating and drinking before others because the need to explain its issues is more important than others. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: The milk of a she-ass is disliked because milk is generated from meat so it becomes like it. Similarly, the milk of horses is disliked according to Imam Abu Hanifa, like its meat according to him. And there is a difference of opinion on the dislike of horse meat according to the two companions; as in Fatawa Qadikhan. The jallalah is not eaten nor its milk drunk because the Prophet, peace be upon him, prohibited eating it and drinking its milk. The jallalah is the animal that habitually eats carrion and does not mix [its diet], so its meat becomes foul. If it is confined until the foulness goes away, it becomes permissible. No period was determined for that in the original [sources], but in al-Nawadir it is determined as one month. It is said: forty days for camels, twenty days for cows, ten days for sheep, and three days for chickens. And the animal that mixes [its diet] by consuming impurity and carrion and also consumes other things in a way that no effect appears in its meat – there is no problem in its being lawful. For this reason, it is permissible to eat a lamb that was fed with pig's milk because its meat does not change, and what it was fed becomes consumed and leaves no trace. For this reason, they said: There is no problem in eating chicken because it mixes [its diet] and its meat does not change. And what is narrated about confining chicken for three days then slaughtering it – that is done as a voluntary act, not as a condition. In al-Muhit: There is no problem in eating barley found in camel or sheep dung; it is washed and eaten. But [barley] found in the innards of cows and horse dung is not eaten because camel dung is hard and impurity does not penetrate the parts of barley and wheat. There is no problem in eating olive worms before the soul is breathed into them because the sin of killing is only attributed to that which has a soul. It is disliked to take exertion from the water-skin and carry it home because it is placed for drinking, not for carrying. And wood found in water – if it has no value, it is lawful because permission is given to take it; if it has value, then not. There is no problem for women to chew gum because their teeth are weaker than men's teeth, so gum is established for them in place of the miswak. There is no problem for women to dye their hands and feet as long as the dye does not contain images. It is disliked for men and boys because that is adornment, and it is permissible for women but not men. There is no problem with dye...
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a chapter (fasl) on the rulings of eating and drinking, presented early due to its everyday importance. The author begins with a statement that the milk of a she-ass is disliked (makruh) because milk derives from the animal's meat, so the ruling on milk follows the ruling on meat. For horse milk, Imam Abu Hanifa considers it disliked like horse meat, but there is an intra-madhhab difference: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad do not consider horse meat disliked, and therefore its milk would not be disliked by them either. This is recorded in Fatawa Qadikhan. Then the ruling on the jallalah animal is given: an animal that feeds mainly on filth and carrion, causing its meat to become foul-smelling. It is prohibited to eat its meat or drink its milk based on a hadith. However, if it is confined and fed clean food until the foulness disappears, it becomes permissible. The original Hanafi sources do not set a specific period, but in al-Nawadir (rare transmissions) a month is stated, and other estimates vary by animal: 40 days for camels, 20 for cows, 10 for sheep, 3 for chickens. If the animal mixes its diet (takhalt) so that the meat does not become foul, it is permissible without confinement. For example, a lamb fed on pig's milk is permissible because the milk is consumed and does not affect the meat. Similarly, chickens are generally permissible because they mix their diet. The report about confining chickens for three days is a recommended practice, not a condition. Next, the passage discusses barley found in camel or sheep dung: since camel dung is hard and dry, the barley remains dry and can be washed and eaten. But barley in cow innards or horse dung is not eaten because those are moist and impurity penetrates. Olive worms are permissible to eat before they develop a soul, as killing is only a sin for sentient beings. It is disliked to use a water-skin for carrying water home because it is meant for drinking from. Wood found in water is permissible to take if it has no value; if it has value, it is not permissible without permission. Chewing gum for women is permissible as a substitute for miswak due to their weaker teeth. Women may dye their hands and feet as long as there are no images (tamathil). Dyeing is disliked for men and boys as it is adornment, which is permissible for women but not for men.

Key Terms
الأتان (al-atan): A female donkey (she-ass). Its milk is disliked because it follows the ruling of its meat.
الخيل (al-khayl): Horses. There is ikhtilaf on the ruling of horse meat: Imam Abu Hanifa says makruh, the Sahibayn say halal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is she-ass milk disliked?
A) Because the Prophet specifically prohibited it.
B) Because milk is generated from meat, and she-ass meat is haram.
C) Because it is harmful to health.
D) Because the Sahibayn said so.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the ruling on milk is derived by qiyas from the ruling on the animal's meat.

The Jallalah Animal: Definition, Prohibition, and Purification
Ruling on jallalah animal and purification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a jallalah animal, state the ruling on eating it and drinking its milk, and explain the conditions for it to become permissible.

Core Idea
A jallalah animal is prohibited until confined until foulness disappears.

Animals that habitually feed on carrion may develop foul meat and milk. The Hanafi school, based on a hadith, prohibits consuming such animals until they are purified by confinement. This lesson details the definition, prohibition, and the varying confinement periods.

Guided Path
1
Define jallalah
A jallalah is an animal that habitually eats carrion (jifah) and does not mix its diet, causing its meat to become foul-smelling.
The term comes from 'jallah' meaning filth.
2
State the general ruling
It is prohibited to eat its meat or drink its milk. The evidence is a hadith: 'The Prophet forbade eating it and drinking its milk.'
The hadith is ahad but accepted.
3
Explain the purification method
If the animal is confined and fed clean food until the foul smell disappears, it becomes permissible. The original sources do not specify a period, but al-Nawadir sets one month. Other estimates vary by animal.
No fixed period in the original; these are ijtihadi.
4
Distinguish mixed diet jallalah
If the animal mixes its diet (e.g., eats both clean and impure food) such that its meat does not become foul, it is permissible without confinement.
Example: chickens are considered mixed-diet and thus permissible.
5
Apply to examples
The passage gives examples: a lamb fed pig's milk is permissible because the milk is consumed and leaves no trace; chickens are permissible because they mix their diet. Confinement for three days (mentioned for chicken) is recommended, not required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It is disliked for men and boys because that is adornment, and it is permissible for women but not men. There is no problem with dye...
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a chapter (fasl) on the rulings of eating and drinking, presented early due to its everyday importance. The author begins with a statement that the milk of a she-ass is disliked (makruh) because milk derives from the animal's meat, so the ruling on milk follows the ruling on meat. For horse milk, Imam Abu Hanifa considers it disliked like horse meat, but there is an intra-madhhab difference: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad do not consider horse meat disliked, and therefore its milk would not be disliked by them either. This is recorded in Fatawa Qadikhan. Then the ruling on the jallalah animal is given: an animal that feeds mainly on filth and carrion, causing its meat to become foul-smelling. It is prohibited to eat its meat or drink its milk based on a hadith. However, if it is confined and fed clean food until the foulness disappears, it becomes permissible. The original Hanafi sources do not set a specific period, but in al-Nawadir (rare transmissions) a month is stated, and other estimates vary by animal: 40 days for camels, 20 for cows, 10 for sheep, 3 for chickens. If the animal mixes its diet (takhalt) so that the meat does not become foul, it is permissible without confinement. For example, a lamb fed on pig's milk is permissible because the milk is consumed and does not affect the meat. Similarly, chickens are generally permissible because they mix their diet. The report about confining chickens for three days is a recommended practice, not a condition. Next, the passage discusses barley found in camel or sheep dung: since camel dung is hard and dry, the barley remains dry and can be washed and eaten. But barley in cow innards or horse dung is not eaten because those are moist and impurity penetrates. Olive worms are permissible to eat before they develop a soul, as killing is only a sin for sentient beings. It is disliked to use a water-skin for carrying water home because it is meant for drinking from. Wood found in water is permissible to take if it has no value; if it has value, it is not permissible without permission. Chewing gum for women is permissible as a substitute for miswak due to their weaker teeth. Women may dye their hands and feet as long as there are no images (tamathil). Dyeing is disliked for men and boys as it is adornment, which is permissible for women but not for men.

Key Terms
الجلالة (al-jallalah): An animal that habitually eats carrion and does not mix its diet, causing its meat to become foul-smelling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the evidence for the prohibition of jallalah?
A) It is based on qiyas with swine.
B) The Prophet prohibited eating it and drinking its milk.
C) It is prohibited because it is harmful.
D) It is prohibited because of a consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
A jallalah animal becomes permissible after confinement until the foulness disappears, with no fixed period required in the original sources.

Barley Found in Animal Dung: Purity and Consumption
Ruling on barley in dung

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on eating barley found in camel or sheep dung versus cow or horse dung, and explain the reason for the difference.

Core Idea
Barley in dry dung is permissible after washing; in moist dung it is not.

Camel and sheep dung is dry and hard, so barley grains found inside remain relatively clean and can be washed. Cow and horse dung is moist, causing impurity to penetrate the grain, making it impure. This lesson explains the fiqh ruling based on the physical nature of the dung.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Barley grains are found in the dung of various animals. The ruling depends on the type of dung.
2
State the ruling for camel and sheep dung
Barley found in camel or sheep dung is permissible after washing. The dung is hard and dry, so impurity does not penetrate the grain.
The text says 'لا بأس' – no problem.
3
State the ruling for cow and horse dung
Barley found in cow or horse dung is not eaten because the dung is moist and impurity penetrates the grain.
The text says 'لا يؤكل' – it is not eaten.
4
Understand the reasoning
The difference is due to the consistency of the dung: hard and dry vs. moist. In hard dung, the grain remains free from impurity; in moist dung, the impurity seeps in.
This is a ruling based on physical reality (haqiqah).

Source Text

فصل في الأكل والشرب
قدم فصل الأكل والشرب على غيره لأن الاحتياج إلى بيان مسائله أهم من غيره. قال رحمه الله : (كره لبن (الأتان لأن اللبن يتولد من اللحم فصار مثله، وكذا لبن الخيل يكره عند الإمام كلحمه عنده، واختلف في كراهة لحم الخيل عندهما ؛ كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان. ولا تؤكل الجلالة ولا يشرب لبنها لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام نهى عن أكلها وشرب لبنها. التي تعتاد أكل الجيف ولا تخلط فيكون لحمها منتناً، ولو حبست حتى يزول والجلالة هي النتن حلت ولم يقدر لذلك مدة في الأصل، وقدر في النوادر بشهر، وقيل بأربعين يوماً في الإبل، وبعشرين يوماً في البقر، وبعشرة أيام في الشاة، وثلاثة أيام في الدجاجة. والتي تخلط بأن تتناول النجاسة والجيف وتتناول غيرها على وجه لا يظهر أثر ذلك في لحمها فلا بأس بحلها ولهذا يحل أكل جذع تغذى بلبن الخنزير لأن لحمه لا يتغير وما تغدى به يصير مستهلكاً لا يبقى له أثر ولهذا قالوا : لا بأس بأكل الدجاج لأنها تخلط ولا يتغير لحمه، وما روي أن الدجاج يحبس ثلاثة أيام ثم يذبح ذلك على وجه القربة لا على أنه شرط. وفي المحيط : ولا بأس بأكل شعير يوجد في بعر الإبل والشاة فيغسل ويؤكل، وإن في أحشاء البقر وروث الفرس لا يؤكل لأن البعر صلب فلا تتداخل النجاسة في أجزاء الشعير والحنطة. ولا بأس بأكل دود الزيتون قبل أن تنفخ فيه الروح لأن إثم اميت إنما يطلق على من له روح. ويكره دفع الجهد من السقاية وحمله إلى المنزل لأنه وضع للشرب لا للحمل، والحطب الذي يوجد في الماء إن كان لا قيمة له فهو حلال لأنه مأذون في أخذه، وإن كان له قيمة فلا . ولا بأس بمضغ العلك للنساء لأن سنهن أضعف من سن الرجال فأقيم العلك لهن مقام السواك، ولا بأس للنساء بخضاب اليد والرجل ما لم يكن خضاب فيه تماثيل، ويكره للرجال والصبيان لأن ذلك تزين وهو مباح للنساء دون الرجال. ولا بأس بخضاب

البعر (al-ba'r): The dung of camels and sheep; it is hard and dry.
الروث (al-rawth): The dung of horses and similar animals; it is moist.

Olive Worms Before the Soul: Permissibility of Eating
Ruling on eating olive worms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on eating olive worms and explain the rationale based on the presence of a soul.

Core Idea
Eating olive worms before they have a soul is permissible.

The Hanafi school rules that it is permissible to eat olive worms found in olives before the soul is breathed into them. The prohibition of killing only applies to creatures with a soul (ruh). This lesson explains the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Olive worms (dud al-zaytun) are small creatures that develop inside olives.
2
State the ruling
There is no problem (la ba'sa) in eating them before the soul is breathed into them.
The time 'before soul' refers to early development stages.
3
Understand the rationale
The prohibition of killing (ithm) only applies to that which has a soul. Since the worm does not yet have a soul, eating it is not sinful.
This is based on the Islamic principle that life is respected only after ensoulment.

Source Text

Using a Water-Skin for Carrying: Disliked Action
Ruling on using water-skin for carrying

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on using a water-skin (siqaya) for carrying water home and explain the reason.

Core Idea
A water-skin is meant for drinking, not carrying; using it for carrying is disliked.

Water-skins (siqaya) were traditionally used for drinking water on journeys. The Hanafi school rules that it is disliked (makruh) to use them for carrying water home because that is not their intended purpose. This lesson explains the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object
A water-skin (siqaya) is a leather bag used for storing and drinking water.
2
State the ruling
It is disliked (yukrah) to take exertion from the water-skin (i.e., use it to carry water) and carry it home.
The phrase 'دفع الجهد من السقاية' means to exert effort with the water-skin beyond its purpose.
3
Understand the reason
The water-skin is designed for drinking (shurb), not for carrying (haml). Using it for carrying disrespects its intended function.
This is a matter of proper use of resources.

Source Text

فصل في الأكل والشرب
قدم فصل الأكل والشرب على غيره لأن الاحتياج إلى بيان مسائله أهم من غيره. قال رحمه الله : (كره لبن (الأتان لأن اللبن يتولد من اللحم فصار مثله، وكذا لبن الخيل يكره عند الإمام كلحمه عنده، واختلف في كراهة لحم الخيل عندهما ؛ كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان. ولا تؤكل الجلالة ولا يشرب لبنها لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام نهى عن أكلها وشرب لبنها. التي تعتاد أكل الجيف ولا تخلط فيكون لحمها منتناً، ولو حبست حتى يزول والجلالة هي النتن حلت ولم يقدر لذلك مدة في الأصل، وقدر في النوادر بشهر، وقيل بأربعين يوماً في الإبل، وبعشرين يوماً في البقر، وبعشرة أيام في الشاة، وثلاثة أيام في الدجاجة. والتي تخلط بأن تتناول النجاسة والجيف وتتناول غيرها على وجه لا يظهر أثر ذلك في لحمها فلا بأس بحلها ولهذا يحل أكل جذع تغذى بلبن الخنزير لأن لحمه لا يتغير وما تغدى به يصير مستهلكاً لا يبقى له أثر ولهذا قالوا : لا بأس بأكل الدجاج لأنها تخلط ولا يتغير لحمه، وما روي أن الدجاج يحبس ثلاثة أيام ثم يذبح ذلك على وجه القربة لا على أنه شرط. وفي المحيط : ولا بأس بأكل شعير يوجد في بعر الإبل والشاة فيغسل ويؤكل، وإن في أحشاء البقر وروث الفرس لا يؤكل لأن البعر صلب فلا تتداخل النجاسة في أجزاء الشعير والحنطة. ولا بأس بأكل دود الزيتون قبل أن تنفخ فيه الروح لأن إثم اميت إنما يطلق على من له روح. ويكره دفع الجهد من السقاية وحمله إلى المنزل لأنه وضع للشرب لا للحمل، والحطب الذي يوجد في الماء إن كان لا قيمة له فهو حلال لأنه مأذون في أخذه، وإن كان له قيمة فلا . ولا بأس بمضغ العلك للنساء لأن سنهن أضعف من سن الرجال فأقيم العلك لهن مقام السواك، ولا بأس للنساء بخضاب اليد والرجل ما لم يكن خضاب فيه تماثيل، ويكره للرجال والصبيان لأن ذلك تزين وهو مباح للنساء دون الرجال. ولا بأس بخضاب
Chapter on Eating and Drinking. He placed the chapter on eating and drinking before others because the need to explain its issues is more important than others. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: The milk of a she-ass is disliked because milk is generated from meat so it becomes like it. Similarly, the milk of horses is disliked according to Imam Abu Hanifa, like its meat according to him. And there is a difference of opinion on the dislike of horse meat according to the two companions; as in Fatawa Qadikhan. The jallalah is not eaten nor its milk drunk because the Prophet, peace be upon him, prohibited eating it and drinking its milk. The jallalah is the animal that habitually eats carrion and does not mix [its diet], so its meat becomes foul. If it is confined until the foulness goes away, it becomes permissible. No period was determined for that in the original [sources], but in al-Nawadir it is determined as one month. It is said: forty days for camels, twenty days for cows, ten days for sheep, and three days for chickens. And the animal that mixes [its diet] by consuming impurity and carrion and also consumes other things in a way that no effect appears in its meat – there is no problem in its being lawful. For this reason, it is permissible to eat a lamb that was fed with pig's milk because its meat does not change, and what it was fed becomes consumed and leaves no trace. For this reason, they said: There is no problem in eating chicken because it mixes [its diet] and its meat does not change. And what is narrated about confining chicken for three days then slaughtering it – that is done as a voluntary act, not as a condition. In al-Muhit: There is no problem in eating barley found in camel or sheep dung; it is washed and eaten. But [barley] found in the innards of cows and horse dung is not eaten because camel dung is hard and impurity does not penetrate the parts of barley and wheat. There is no problem in eating olive worms before the soul is breathed into them because the sin of killing is only attributed to that which has a soul. It is disliked to take exertion from the water-skin and carry it home because it is placed for drinking, not for carrying. And wood found in water – if it has no value, it is lawful because permission is given to take it; if it has value, then not. There is no problem for women to chew gum because their teeth are weaker than men's teeth, so gum is established for them in place of the miswak. There is no problem for women to dye their hands and feet as long as the dye does not contain images. It is disliked for men and boys because that is adornment, and it is permissible for women but not men. There is no problem with dye...
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a chapter (fasl) on the rulings of eating and drinking, presented early due to its everyday importance. The author begins with a statement that the milk of a she-ass is disliked (makruh) because milk derives from the animal's meat, so the ruling on milk follows the ruling on meat. For horse milk, Imam Abu Hanifa considers it disliked like horse meat, but there is an intra-madhhab difference: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad do not consider horse meat disliked, and therefore its milk would not be disliked by them either. This is recorded in Fatawa Qadikhan. Then the ruling on the jallalah animal is given: an animal that feeds mainly on filth and carrion, causing its meat to become foul-smelling. It is prohibited to eat its meat or drink its milk based on a hadith. However, if it is confined and fed clean food until the foulness disappears, it becomes permissible. The original Hanafi sources do not set a specific period, but in al-Nawadir (rare transmissions) a month is stated, and other estimates vary by animal: 40 days for camels, 20 for cows, 10 for sheep, 3 for chickens. If the animal mixes its diet (takhalt) so that the meat does not become foul, it is permissible without confinement. For example, a lamb fed on pig's milk is permissible because the milk is consumed and does not affect the meat. Similarly, chickens are generally permissible because they mix their diet. The report about confining chickens for three days is a recommended practice, not a condition. Next, the passage discusses barley found in camel or sheep dung: since camel dung is hard and dry, the barley remains dry and can be washed and eaten. But barley in cow innards or horse dung is not eaten because those are moist and impurity penetrates. Olive worms are permissible to eat before they develop a soul, as killing is only a sin for sentient beings. It is disliked to use a water-skin for carrying water home because it is meant for drinking from. Wood found in water is permissible to take if it has no value; if it has value, it is not permissible without permission. Chewing gum for women is permissible as a substitute for miswak due to their weaker teeth. Women may dye their hands and feet as long as there are no images (tamathil). Dyeing is disliked for men and boys as it is adornment, which is permissible for women but not for men.

Key Terms
السقاية (al-siqaya): A water-skin, typically used for drinking water during travel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it disliked to carry a water-skin home?
A) Because it may leak.
B) Because it is heavy.
C) Because it is intended for drinking, not for carrying.
D) Because the Prophet forbade it.

Key Takeaway
Using an object for something other than its intended purpose is disliked in Hanafi fiqh.

Wood Found in Water: Ownership and Permission
Ruling on taking wood from water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on taking wood found in water based on its value and explain the reasoning.

Core Idea
If wood is valueless, it is permissible; if valuable, it is not.

Wood found floating in water may be considered ownerless or owned. The Hanafi school rules that if the wood has no value, it can be taken without permission. If it has value, it is not permissible because it likely belongs to someone.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object
Wood (hatab) found in water, such as driftwood.
2
State the ruling based on value
If the wood has no value (la qeemata lahu), it is halal (permissible) to take it because general permission is given. If it has value, it is not permissible to take it without owner's consent.
Value is determined by customary standards.
3
Understand the reason
Valueless wood is considered ownerless (mubah); valuable wood is owned and protected.

Source Text

Chewing Gum for Women: Permissible as a Teeth Cleaner
Ruling on women chewing gum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on women chewing gum and explain the reasoning based on dental health.

Core Idea
Women may chew gum as a substitute for miswak due to weaker teeth.

Chewing gum (ʿilk) was used for dental hygiene. The Hanafi school permits it for women because their teeth are generally weaker than men's, so gum is established as a suitable alternative to the miswak. This lesson explains the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
Chewing gum (ʿilk) to clean teeth.
2
State the ruling
There is no problem (la ba'sa) for women to chew gum.
3
Understand the reason
Women's teeth are weaker than men's, so gum serves as a substitute for miswak, which men typically use.
This is a concession due to physical difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
What is the reason given for allowing women to chew gum?
A) It is a type of food.

Dyeing (Khidab) for Women and Men: Permissibility and Dislike
Ruling on dyeing hands and feet

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on dyeing hands and feet for women and men, including the condition of no images.

Core Idea
Women may dye their hands and feet without images; men and boys may not.

Dyeing (khidab) using henna or similar is a form of adornment. The Hanafi school permits it for women as long as there are no images. For men and boys, it is disliked because it is considered beautification inappropriate for them. This lesson explains the gender distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
Dyeing (khidab) of hands and feet, traditionally with henna.
2
State the ruling for women
There is no problem (la ba'sa) for women to dye their hands and feet, provided the dye does not contain images (tamathil).
Images refer to figures of living beings.
3
State the ruling for men and boys
It is disliked (yukrah) for men and boys because it is adornment, which is permissible for women but not for men.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأكل والشرب
قدم فصل الأكل والشرب على غيره لأن الاحتياج إلى بيان مسائله أهم من غيره. قال رحمه الله : (كره لبن (الأتان لأن اللبن يتولد من اللحم فصار مثله، وكذا لبن الخيل يكره عند الإمام كلحمه عنده، واختلف في كراهة لحم الخيل عندهما ؛ كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان. ولا تؤكل الجلالة ولا يشرب لبنها لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام نهى عن أكلها وشرب لبنها. التي تعتاد أكل الجيف ولا تخلط فيكون لحمها منتناً، ولو حبست حتى يزول والجلالة هي النتن حلت ولم يقدر لذلك مدة في الأصل، وقدر في النوادر بشهر، وقيل بأربعين يوماً في الإبل، وبعشرين يوماً في البقر، وبعشرة أيام في الشاة، وثلاثة أيام في الدجاجة. والتي تخلط بأن تتناول النجاسة والجيف وتتناول غيرها على وجه لا يظهر أثر ذلك في لحمها فلا بأس بحلها ولهذا يحل أكل جذع تغذى بلبن الخنزير لأن لحمه لا يتغير وما تغدى به يصير مستهلكاً لا يبقى له أثر ولهذا قالوا : لا بأس بأكل الدجاج لأنها تخلط ولا يتغير لحمه، وما روي أن الدجاج يحبس ثلاثة أيام ثم يذبح ذلك على وجه القربة لا على أنه شرط. وفي المحيط : ولا بأس بأكل شعير يوجد في بعر الإبل والشاة فيغسل ويؤكل، وإن في أحشاء البقر وروث الفرس لا يؤكل لأن البعر صلب فلا تتداخل النجاسة في أجزاء الشعير والحنطة. ولا بأس بأكل دود الزيتون قبل أن تنفخ فيه الروح لأن إثم اميت إنما يطلق على من له روح. ويكره دفع الجهد من السقاية وحمله إلى المنزل لأنه وضع للشرب لا للحمل، والحطب الذي يوجد في الماء إن كان لا قيمة له فهو حلال لأنه مأذون في أخذه، وإن كان له قيمة فلا . ولا بأس بمضغ العلك للنساء لأن سنهن أضعف من سن الرجال فأقيم العلك لهن مقام السواك، ولا بأس للنساء بخضاب اليد والرجل ما لم يكن خضاب فيه تماثيل، ويكره للرجال والصبيان لأن ذلك تزين وهو مباح للنساء دون الرجال. ولا بأس بخضاب
Chapter on Eating and Drinking. He placed the chapter on eating and drinking before others because the need to explain its issues is more important than others. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: The milk of a she-ass is disliked because milk is generated from meat so it becomes like it. Similarly, the milk of horses is disliked according to Imam Abu Hanifa, like its meat according to him. And there is a difference of opinion on the dislike of horse meat according to the two companions; as in Fatawa Qadikhan. The jallalah is not eaten nor its milk drunk because the Prophet, peace be upon him, prohibited eating it and drinking its milk. The jallalah is the animal that habitually eats carrion and does not mix [its diet], so its meat becomes foul. If it is confined until the foulness goes away, it becomes permissible. No period was determined for that in the original [sources], but in al-Nawadir it is determined as one month. It is said: forty days for camels, twenty days for cows, ten days for sheep, and three days for chickens. And the animal that mixes [its diet] by consuming impurity and carrion and also consumes other things in a way that no effect appears in its meat – there is no problem in its being lawful. For this reason, it is permissible to eat a lamb that was fed with pig's milk because its meat does not change, and what it was fed becomes consumed and leaves no trace. For this reason, they said: There is no problem in eating chicken because it mixes [its diet] and its meat does not change. And what is narrated about confining chicken for three days then slaughtering it – that is done as a voluntary act, not as a condition. In al-Muhit: There is no problem in eating barley found in camel or sheep dung; it is washed and eaten. But [barley] found in the innards of cows and horse dung is not eaten because camel dung is hard and impurity does not penetrate the parts of barley and wheat. There is no problem in eating olive worms before the soul is breathed into them because the sin of killing is only attributed to that which has a soul. It is disliked to take exertion from the water-skin and carry it home because it is placed for drinking, not for carrying. And wood found in water – if it has no value, it is lawful because permission is given to take it; if it has value, then not. There is no problem for women to chew gum because their teeth are weaker than men's teeth, so gum is established for them in place of the miswak. There is no problem for women to dye their hands and feet as long as the dye does not contain images. It is disliked for men and boys because that is adornment, and it is permissible for women but not men. There is no problem with dye...
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a chapter (fasl) on the rulings of eating and drinking, presented early due to its everyday importance. The author begins with a statement that the milk of a she-ass is disliked (makruh) because milk derives from the animal's meat, so the ruling on milk follows the ruling on meat. For horse milk, Imam Abu Hanifa considers it disliked like horse meat, but there is an intra-madhhab difference: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad do not consider horse meat disliked, and therefore its milk would not be disliked by them either. This is recorded in Fatawa Qadikhan. Then the ruling on the jallalah animal is given: an animal that feeds mainly on filth and carrion, causing its meat to become foul-smelling. It is prohibited to eat its meat or drink its milk based on a hadith. However, if it is confined and fed clean food until the foulness disappears, it becomes permissible. The original Hanafi sources do not set a specific period, but in al-Nawadir (rare transmissions) a month is stated, and other estimates vary by animal: 40 days for camels, 20 for cows, 10 for sheep, 3 for chickens. If the animal mixes its diet (takhalt) so that the meat does not become foul, it is permissible without confinement. For example, a lamb fed on pig's milk is permissible because the milk is consumed and does not affect the meat. Similarly, chickens are generally permissible because they mix their diet. The report about confining chickens for three days is a recommended practice, not a condition. Next, the passage discusses barley found in camel or sheep dung: since camel dung is hard and dry, the barley remains dry and can be washed and eaten. But barley in cow innards or horse dung is not eaten because those are moist and impurity penetrates. Olive worms are permissible to eat before they develop a soul, as killing is only a sin for sentient beings. It is disliked to use a water-skin for carrying water home because it is meant for drinking from. Wood found in water is permissible to take if it has no value; if it has value, it is not permissible without permission. Chewing gum for women is permissible as a substitute for miswak due to their weaker teeth. Women may dye their hands and feet as long as there are no images (tamathil). Dyeing is disliked for men and boys as it is adornment, which is permissible for women but not for men.

Key Terms
الخضاب (al-khidab): Dyeing hands and feet, typically with henna.
التماثيل (al-tamathil): Images or figures, especially of living beings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
For whom is dyeing hands and feet disliked according to this passage?
A) Women only.
B) Men and boys.
C) Everyone.
D) Only old men.

Key Takeaway
Dyeing (khidab) is permissible for women without images, but disliked for men and boys as adornment.

Ruling on Donkey and Horse Milk
Ruling on donkey and horse milk, with intra-madhhab ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on drinking donkey and horse milk and identify the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf regarding horse milk.

Core Idea
Milk takes the ruling of the meat: donkey milk is makruh; horse milk is disputed but the fatwa is permissibility.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the rulings on milks from donkeys and horses. The key principle is that milk is generated from flesh, so its ruling follows that of the meat. Understanding this principle and the existing scholarly difference within the Hanafi school will equip you to navigate similar cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the animals and their milks
The passage discusses milk of the female donkey (أتأن) and horse (خيل). Note the general principle: milk follows the ruling of the flesh.
2
Apply the principle to donkey milk
Since donkey flesh is disliked (makruh) in the Hanafi school, its milk is also makruh.
No specific hadith is cited; the ruling is based on qiyas.
3
Identify the ikhtilaf on horse milk
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that horse milk is makruh, like its meat. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) disagree and consider it permissible.
The passage cites Fatawa Qadikhan for this ikhtilaf.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon fatwa is permissibility, following the Sahibayn. Thus, drinking horse milk is halal.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كره لبن الأتان لأن اللبن يتولد من اللحم فصار مثله، وكذا لبن الخيل يكره عند الإمام كلحمه عنده، واختلف في كراهة لحم الخيل عندهما ؛ كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان. ولا تؤكل الجلالة ولا يشرب لبنها لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام نهى عن أكلها وشرب لبنها. التي تعتاد أكل الجيف ولا تخلط فيكون لحمها منتناً، ولو حبست حتى يزول والجلالة هي النتن حلت ولم يقدر لذلك مدة في الأصل، وقدر في النوادر بشهر، وقيل بأربعين يوماً في الإبل، وبعشرين يوماً في البقر، وبعشرة أيام في الشاة، وثلاثة أيام في الدجاجة. والتي تخلط بأن تتناول النجاسة والجيف وتتناول غيرها على وجه لا يظهر أثر ذلك في لحمها فلا بأس بحلها ولهذا يحل أكل جذع تغذى بلبن الخنزير لأن لحمه لا يتغير وما تغدى به يصير مستهلكاً لا يبقى له أثر ولهذا قالوا : لا بأس بأكل الدجاج لأنها تخلط ولا يتغير لحمه، وما روي أن الدجاج يحبس ثلاثة أيام ثم يذبح ذلك على وجه القربة لا على أنه شرط.
Donkey milk is disliked because milk is generated from flesh so it becomes like it. Similarly, horse milk is disliked according to Imam Abu Hanifa, as is its flesh according to him, and there is disagreement regarding the dislike of horse flesh between the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). As in Fatawa Qadikhan. And the jallala (animal that eats filth) is not eaten nor its milk drunk because the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade eating it and drinking its milk. The jallala is the animal that habitually eats carrion and does not mix (with clean food) so its flesh becomes putrid. If it is confined until the putrid smell goes away, it becomes permissible. No specific period is fixed in the original (books), but in the Nawadir it is set as a month, and it is said forty days for camels, twenty days for cows, ten days for sheep, and three days for chickens. And the one that mixes (by eating both filth and clean food) in a way that the effect does not appear in its flesh, then there is no harm in eating it. For this reason, it is permissible to eat a lamb fed on pig's milk because its flesh does not change, and what it is fed with becomes consumed and has no trace. Therefore they said: There is no harm in eating chickens because they mix and their flesh does not change. And what is narrated that chickens should be confined for three days then slaughtered—that is on the basis of piety, not as a condition.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with rulings regarding the milk of donkeys and horses, and the concept of jallala (animals that habitually eat impure substances). The author states that drinking donkey milk is disliked (makruh) because milk is derived from flesh, and donkey flesh itself is disliked. For horse milk, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, it is also disliked, paralleling the ruling on horse meat. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on the dislike of horse meat, though the relied-upon position is that it is permissible. The text then addresses jallala: an animal that eats carrion and does not eat clean food, causing its flesh to become putrid. Such an animal is prohibited to eat or drink its milk, based on a hadith forbidding it. If the animal is confined until the putrid smell disappears, it becomes lawful. The original sources (usul) do not specify a period, but the Nawadir (rare transmissions) set a month, with specific durations for different animals: 40 days for camels, 20 for cows, 10 for sheep, 3 for chickens. If the animal mixes clean and impure food such that its flesh does not show effects, it is permissible without confinement. An example: a lamb fed pig's milk is lawful because the milk is consumed and leaves no trace. Similarly, chickens that mix foods are permissible; the narration about confining chickens for three days is recommended, not obligatory.

Key Terms
اللبن (al-laban): Milk, considered like the flesh of the animal in terms of ruling
الكراهة (al-karahah): Dislike, a legal category between permissible and prohibited

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon Hanafi fatwa (mufta bihi) regarding horse milk?
A) It is haram.
B) It is makruh tanzihi.
C) It is permissible.
D) It is makruh tahrimi.

Key Takeaway
Milk follows the flesh: donkey milk is makruh; horse milk is permissible according to the relied-upon fatwa.

Ruling on Jallala Animals
Definition, prohibition, and conditions for jallala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define jallala, state its prohibition based on hadith, and apply the conditions that make it permissible.

Core Idea
A jallala (habitual filth-eater) is haram until confinement removes its putrid smell; durations vary by animal.

This passage continues with the important concept of 'jallala' – an animal that primarily eats carrion and filth, causing its flesh to become putrid. The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade eating such animals and drinking their milk. However, the Hanafi school provides a clear remedy: confinement until the foul odor disappears. The text also distinguishes animals that mix clean and impure food, which do not require confinement.

Guided Path
1
Define jallala
A jallala is an animal that habitually eats carrion (جيف) and does not mix clean food, so its flesh becomes putrid.
The term comes from الجلة (dung/filth).
2
State the ruling and its proof
It is haram to eat the meat or drink the milk of such an animal. The proof is the hadith: the Prophet forbade eating it and drinking its milk.
The hadith is cited as a proof: 'نهى عن أكلها وشرب لبنها'.
3
Identify the condition for permissibility
If the animal is confined (حبست) until the putrid smell (النتن) disappears, it becomes halal.
No fixed period in original texts; Nawadir specifies durations.
4
Note the durations from Nawadir
Nawadir (rare transmissions) prescribe: camels 40 days, cows 20, sheep 10, chickens 3. The original books do not set a period; confinement until smell disappears suffices.
These are recommended, not obligatory.
5
Distinguish mixing animals
If the animal eats both filth and clean food such that no effect appears in its flesh, it is permissible without confinement. Example: lamb fed pig's milk is permissible because the milk is consumed.
The narration about confining chickens for 3 days is recommended piety, not a condition.
