Donkey milk is disliked because milk is generated from flesh so it becomes like it. Similarly, horse milk is disliked according to Imam Abu Hanifa, as is its flesh according to him, and there is disagreement regarding the dislike of horse flesh between the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). As in Fatawa Qadikhan. And the jallala (animal that eats filth) is not eaten nor its milk drunk because the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade eating it and drinking its milk. The jallala is the animal that habitually eats carrion and does not mix (with clean food) so its flesh becomes putrid. If it is confined until the putrid smell goes away, it becomes permissible. No specific period is fixed in the original (books), but in the Nawadir it is set as a month, and it is said forty days for camels, twenty days for cows, ten days for sheep, and three days for chickens. And the one that mixes (by eating both filth and clean food) in a way that the effect does not appear in its flesh, then there is no harm in eating it. For this reason, it is permissible to eat a lamb fed on pig's milk because its flesh does not change, and what it is fed with becomes consumed and has no trace. Therefore they said: There is no harm in eating chickens because they mix and their flesh does not change. And what is narrated that chickens should be confined for three days then slaughtered—that is on the basis of piety, not as a condition.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with rulings regarding the milk of donkeys and horses, and the concept of jallala (animals that habitually eat impure substances). The author states that drinking donkey milk is disliked (makruh) because milk is derived from flesh, and donkey flesh itself is disliked. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on the dislike of horse meat, though the relied-upon position is that it is permissible. An example: a lamb fed pig's milk is lawful because the milk is consumed and leaves no trace. Similarly, chickens that mix foods are permissible; the narration about confining chickens for three days is recommended, not obligatory.

Key Terms
الجلالة (al-jallala): Animal that habitually eats carrion and filth, causing its flesh to become putrid
النوادر (al-nawadir): Rare transmissions in the Hanafi school, distinguished from zahir al-riwayah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minimum requirement for a jallala animal to become permissible according to the original sources (usul)?
A) Confinement for exactly one month.
B) Confinement until the putrid smell disappears.
C) Confinement for 40 days for camels.
D) It is always haram.

Key Takeaway
A jallala animal is haram only as long as its flesh is putrid; confinement until the smell disappears renders it halal, with recommended durations from Nawadir.

Unusual Foods and Public Resources: Hanafi Rulings
Rulings on unusual foods and taking public resources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on barley in dung, worms in olives, and taking water/firewood from public sources, and explain the rationale (ta'lil) for each.

Core Idea
Permissibility depends on impurity penetration, ensoulment of creatures, and intended use of public resources.

This passage from al-Muhit presents three distinct but practical rulings: barley found in animal dung, worms in olives, and taking water or firewood from public places. Each ruling is based on a clear rationale: impurity transfer, ensoulment, and the purpose of public amenities. Let us walk through each case to understand the conditions and the legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify each item and its ruling
Read the passage and list the three items: barley in dung, worms in olives, water from siqayah, firewood from water. Note the ruling for each: halal (after washing), halal (before soul), makruh (to carry home), halal (if no value) / not permissible (if value).
2
Understand the ta'lil for barley in dung
The passage explains that camel and sheep dung is solid (ba'r salb), so impurity does not penetrate barley grains. Washing suffices. In contrast, cow intestines and horse manure are softer, so impurity penetrates, making the barley impermissible.
The key distinction is solid vs. soft dung.
3
Understand the ta'lil for worms in olives
Worms before the soul is breathed are considered non-ensouled. The sin of killing only applies to ensouled creatures. Therefore, eating them is permissible. After ensoulment, they would be impermissible.
Hanafi position: only ensouled beings trigger prohibition of killing.
4
Understand the ta'lil for water and firewood
The siqayah (public drinking place) is established for immediate drinking, not for carrying away. Taking effort to carry water home is makruh because it misuses the public facility. Firewood found without value is ownerless and permissible; with value, it belongs to someone and cannot be taken without permission.
The ruling depends on the purpose of the resource and ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : ولا بأس بأكل شعير يوجد في بعر الإبل والشاة فيغسل ويؤكل، وإن في أحشاء البقر وروث الفرس لا يؤكل لأن البعر صلب فلا تتداخل النجاسة في أجزاء الشعير والحنطة. ولا بأس بأكل دود الزيتون قبل أن تنفخ فيه الروح لأن إثم اميت إنما يطلق على من له روح. ويكره دفع الجهد من السقاية وحمله إلى المنزل لأنه وضع للشرب لا للحمل، والحطب الذي يوجد في الماء إن كان لا قيمة له فهو حلال لأنه مأذون في أخذه، وإن كان له قيمة فلا .
And in al-Muhit: There is no harm in eating barley found in camel and sheep dung—it is washed and eaten. But that which is in the intestines of cows and horse manure is not eaten because dung is solid, so impurity does not penetrate the parts of barley and wheat. And there is no harm in eating olives' worms before the soul is breathed into them because the sin of killing is only attributed to that which has a soul. And it is disliked to take the effort from the drinking place and carry it home because it is placed for drinking, not for carrying. And firewood found in water: if it has no value, it is permissible because one is permitted to take it; but if it has value, then it is not.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage gives specific rulings on unusual foods and items. Barley found in camel or sheep dung is permissible to eat after washing because the dung is solid and impurity does not penetrate the barley. However, barley found in cow intestines or horse manure is not eaten because those are softer and impurity may have penetrated. Next, eating worms found in olives before they have a soul is permissible; the prohibition of killing applies only to ensouled beings. Then, it is disliked (makruh) to take water from a public drinking place (sagaya) to one's home, as it is meant for immediate drinking, not for carrying away. Firewood found floating in water: if it is worthless, it is permissible to take it; if it has value, it is not permissible, because taking something of value without owner's permission is not allowed.

Key Terms
البعر (al-ba'r): Solid dung of camels and sheep, as opposed to softer manure
دود الزيتون (dud al-zaytun): Worms found in olives before they have a soul; considered non-ensouled
السقاية (al-siqayah): Public drinking fountain or water station

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is barley found in camel dung permissible to eat after washing?
A) Because camel dung is halal
B) Because the dung is solid and impurity does not penetrate
C) Because the barley is cooked
D) Because it is found in a public place

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, permissibility of unusual foods depends on impurity penetration, ensoulment of creatures, and the intended use of public resources.

Chewing Gum and Dyeing: Gender-Based Rulings
Permissibility of chewing gum for women and dyeing rulings for both genders

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on chewing gum and dyeing (khidhab) for men and women, distinguishing the conditions that make each act permissible or disliked.

Core Idea
Chewing gum and hand/foot dye are for women only; head/beard dye is for both genders.

This passage addresses everyday grooming practices that carry different rulings for men and women based on purpose and social context. Understanding these distinctions helps avoid confusion between permissible adornment and disliked imitation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three acts and gender specificity
The passage covers: (1) chewing gum (women only), (2) dyeing hands/feet (women permissible, men disliked), (3) dyeing head/beard (both permissible).
2
Examine the rationale (illah) for each
For gum: women's teeth are weaker, so gum substitutes for miswak. For hand/foot dye: adornment for women, not men. For head/beard dye: increases love between spouses.
The illah determines the ruling.
3
Note conditions and exceptions
Women's hand/foot dye must not contain images. No conditions mentioned for other acts beyond general permissibility.
Absence of conditions implies it is unrestricted.
4
Classify the rulings
Chewing gum for women: mubah. Dyeing hands/feet: mubah for women, makruh for men. Dyeing head/beard: mubah for both.
Makruh here is tanzihi (disliked).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بأس بمضغ العلك للنساء لأن سنهن أضعف من سن الرجال فأقيم العلك لهن مقام السواك، ولا بأس للنساء بخضاب اليد والرجل ما لم يكن خضاب فيه تماثيل، ويكره للرجال والصبيان لأن ذلك تزين وهو مباح للنساء دون الرجال. ولا بأس بخضاب الرأس واللحية بالحناء والوشمة للرجال والنساء لأن ذلك سبب لزيادة الرغبة والمحبة بين الزوجين.
And there is no harm in chewing gum for women because their teeth are weaker than men's teeth, so gum is established for them as a substitute for siwak. And there is no harm for women in dyeing the hand and foot as long as there is no dye containing images. It is disliked for men and boys because that is adornment, and it is permissible for women but not for men. And there is no harm in dyeing the head and beard with henna and tattooing for men and women because that is a cause for increasing desire and love between spouses.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage addresses permissible adornment practices. Chewing gum (mastic resin) is specifically allowed for women because their teeth are more delicate, and gum serves as a substitute for miswak for oral hygiene. Women are also allowed to dye their hands and feet with henna, provided the dye does not contain forbidden images. For men and boys, dyeing hands and feet is disliked (makruh) because it is considered adornment, which is only permissible for women. However, dyeing the head and beard with henna (and even tattooing, which here likely means permanent dye like kohl or similar) is permissible for both men and women because it enhances spousal attraction and is thus encouraged.

Key Terms
العلك (al-'ilk): Chewing gum or mastic resin used for oral hygiene
الخضاب (al-khidhab): Dyeing, typically with henna, for hands, feet, or head
الوشمة (al-washmah): Tattooing or permanent dye, here used on head/beard

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because it is a sign of piety
D) Because it covers grey hair

Key Takeaway
Gender-based rulings in adornment depend on social context and purpose: what is permissible for women may be disliked for men, unless it promotes spousal harmony.

Is Overeating Always Haram? The Ruling of Eating Beyond Fullness and Its Exceptions
Ruling on eating beyond satiety as israf with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general ruling on eating beyond satiety in the Hanafi school and identify the valid exceptions that render it permissible.

Core Idea
Eating beyond fullness is israf (haram), but valid religious or social purposes make it permissible.

You may have heard that overeating is disliked or even forbidden in Islam. The Hanafi school takes a nuanced position: eating beyond satiety is indeed a form of extravagance (israf), which is haram. However, Imam Muhammad and later Hanafi scholars recognized that certain valid objectives—like honoring a guest or preparing for a fast—can lift the prohibition. This lesson examines the proof from the Quran and the reasoning behind the exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is eating beyond the point of fullness (fawq al-shab‘). The general ruling, according to Imam Muhammad, is haram because it constitutes israf (extravagance) in food.
Israf is explicitly prohibited in the Quran.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is Quran 7:31: 'Eat and drink and do not be extravagant.' The prohibition of israf includes excessive eating.
The verse is qat‘i al-thubut but zanni al-dalala, allowing for interpretation.
3
Understand Imam Muhammad's classification
Imam Muhammad lists eating beyond fullness as a type of ifsad al-ta‘am (corruption of food), which is part of israf in food.
This classification appears in the zahir al-riwayah.
4
Note the later scholars' exceptions
Later Hanafi scholars (mutakhkhirun) added exceptions: if there is a valid purpose (gharad sahih), eating beyond fullness becomes permissible. Examples include hosting a guest to avoid embarrassment and eating heavily before a fast to gain strength.
The exceptions are based on istihsan (juristic preference) overriding the general prohibition.
5
Apply the istihsan reasoning
The general prohibition is overridden by a stronger need: either social obligation (guest rights) or religious preparation (fasting). The principle is that necessity or valid purpose can lift the ruling of israf.
This is a classic example of istihsan in mu‘amalat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية وإن أكل أكثر من حاجته ليتقاياً قال الحسن البصري: رأيت أنس بن مالك يأكل ألواناً من الطعام ويكثر ثم يتقايأ وينفع ذلك المذهب عند أصحابنا . روي عن بعض الأطباء أنه قيل له هل يجد الطبيب في كتاب الله دليل تطب؟ قال : نعم قد جمع الله الطب في هذه الآية وهو قوله كلوا واشربوا ولا تسرفوا [الأعراف: ۳۱] يعني الإسراف في الأكل والشرب هو الذي منه الأمراض. وقيل : كان الرجل قليل الأكل كان أصح جسماً وأجود حفظاً وأذكى فهماً وأقل نوماً وأخف نفساً. ذكر محمد كل واحد منها من إفساد الطعام قال : ومن الإفساد الإسراف في الطعام وهو أنواع؛ فمن ذلك أن يأكل فوق الشبع فهو حرام. ومن المتأخرين من استثنى حالة ما إذا كان له غرض صحيح في الأكل فوق الشبع فيحنئذ لا بأس به، فإن أتاه ضيف بعدما أكل قدر حاجته فليأكل لأجله حتى لا يخجل أو يريد صوم الغد فليتناول فوق الشبع. ومن الإسراف في الطعام الإسراف في المباحات والألوان فذلك منهي عنه إلا عند الحاجة بأن يمل من ناحية واحدة فليستكثر من المباحات ليستوفي من أي لون شاء فيحصل له مقدار ما يتقوى به على الطاعة، وكذلك إذا كان من قصده أن يدعو الأضياف قوماً . بعد قوم إلى أن يأتوا إلى آخر الطعام فلا بأس بالاستكثار في هذه الصورة.
And in al-Zahiriyya: If a person eats more than his need in order to vomit, al-Hasan al-Basri said: I saw Anas ibn Malik eating varieties of food and eating much, then vomiting, and that benefits the madhhab according to our companions. It is narrated from some doctors that it was said to him: Does the doctor find in the Book of Allah a proof for medicine? He said: Yes, Allah has collected medicine in this verse: 'Eat and drink and do not be extravagant' (al-A'raf 31), meaning that extravagance in eating and drinking is the source of diseases. And it was said: The person who eats little has a healthier body, better memory, sharper intellect, less sleep, and lighter soul. Muhammad mentioned each of them from the corruption of food. He said: Among the corruption is extravagance in food, and it is of types. Among them is eating beyond satiety, which is haram. And among the later scholars, one excepted the case when he has a valid purpose in eating beyond satiety, then it is not objectionable. If a guest visits him after he has eaten his fill, he should eat for his sake so that the guest is not embarrassed, or if he intends to fast tomorrow, he may eat beyond satiety. And among extravagance in food is extravagance in permissible things and variety; that is prohibited except when needed. If he becomes bored with one type, he may increase permissible types to choose any he likes, so that he gets enough strength for obedience. Similarly, if his intention is to invite guests group after group until they come to the end of the food, there is no harm in increasing in this case.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on eating beyond satiety. The Hanafi school considers it a form of extravagance (israf), which is haram. The text begins with a report from al-Hasan al-Basri seeing Anas ibn Malik eat much and then vomit, which is cited as a practice that is beneficially allowed in the madhhab for certain purposes. Then a medical perspective is given: the Quranic verse 'Eat and drink and do not be extravagant' encompasses all medical advice, as excess leads to disease. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani classifies extravagance as corruption of food, and among its types is eating beyond fullness—this is haram. However, later scholars made exceptions: if there is a valid religious or social purpose, it becomes permissible. Examples include eating more to spare a guest from embarrassment, or eating more the night before a fast to strengthen oneself. Additionally, extravagant variety in permissible foods is prohibited unless one needs to prevent boredom or to host successive guests. In such cases, it is permissible to prepare a variety.

Key Terms
الإسراف (al-israf): Extravagance, especially in food and drink, beyond what is needed
فوق الشبع (fawq al-shab'): Eating beyond satiety, beyond the point of fullness

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the food is very tasty
B) When one intends to fast the next day
C) When one is eating alone
D) When one is eating vegetables only

Key Takeaway
Eating beyond fullness is generally haram, but valid religious or social purposes such as hosting a guest or preparing for a fast make it permissible.

Extravagance in Eating Bread: Crust, Middle, and Fallen Morsels
Ruling statement on eating bread acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on eating only the middle of bread, discarding the crust, choosing one loaf over another, and picking up fallen morsels.

Core Idea
Wasting bread crust is israf unless someone else eats it; a fallen morsel must be picked up.

The Hanafi school emphasizes avoiding waste (israf) even in small matters. This lesson examines specific behaviors around bread: eating only the soft inner part, discarding the crust, and ignoring fallen morsels. These are not merely etiquette but carry legal rulings ranging from permissible to prohibited depending on circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts described
Read the passage and list the three specific acts related to bread: eating only the middle, discarding the crust, and leaving a fallen morsel.
Also note the mention of choosing one loaf over another.
2
State the default ruling
Determine the default ruling for each act as per the text: 'from extravagance' indicates prohibition (haram) when wasteful.
israf is generally prohibited.
3
Identify exceptions
Find the conditions that change the ruling: if others eat the crust, then eating only the middle is not israf; if the fallen morsel is picked up, it is recommended.
The exception for choosing one loaf over another is always permissible.
4
Derive the practical ruling
Combine the default and exceptions to formulate the operative ruling: discarding crust with no one to eat it is haram; picking up fallen morsel is mustahab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الإسراف أن يأكل وسط الخبز ويدع حواشيه ويأكل ما انتفخ من الخبز كما يفعله بعض الجهال ويزعمون أن ذلك ألذ ولكن هذا إذا كان لا يأكل غيره ما ترك من حواشيه فأما إذا كان غيره يتناول ذلك فلا بأس بذلك كما لا بأس أن يتناول رغيفاً دون رغيف. ومن الإسراف التمسح بالخبز. وفي الذخيرة ومن الإسراف مسح السكين والأصبع بالخبز عند الفراغ من الأكل من غير أن يأكل ما يتمسح فيه، فأما إذا أكل فلا بأس به. وفي التتمة سئل عمن مسح اليد على ثيابه فقال : لا يجوز وسئل عن مسح اليد بدستار ورق فقال : لا يجوز. وفي الكافي : ولا بأس بخرقة الوضوء والمخاط. وفي الجامع الصغير وتكره الخرقة التي تحمل ويمسح بها العرق إلا إذا كان شيئاً لا قيمة له وكذا الخرقة التي يمخط بها، وكذا التي بها الوضوء. وإنما يكره إذا فعل ذلك للتكبر، أما من فعل ذلك للحاجة فلا يكره، ومن الإسراف إذا سقط من يده لقمة أن يتركها بل ينبغي أن يبدأ بتلك اللقمة، وينبغي أن لا يمسح
And among extravagance is eating the middle of the bread and leaving its edges, and eating the puffed part of bread as some ignorant people do, claiming it is more delicious. But that is only if no one else eats the edges he left. If others eat them, then there is no harm, just as there is no harm in taking one loaf over another. And among extravagance is wiping with bread. And in al-Dhakhira: Among extravagance is wiping off the knife and finger with bread after finishing eating without eating what is wiped. If one eats it, there is no harm. And in al-Tatimma: He was asked about one who wiped his hand on his clothes; he said: Not permissible. He was asked about wiping the hand with a towel of paper; he said: Not permissible. And in al-Kafi: There is no harm in a cloth for wudhu and mucus. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: The cloth that is carried and used to wipe sweat is disliked unless it has no value; likewise the cloth used to wipe the nose, and likewise that used for wudhu. It is only disliked if done out of arrogance, but if done out of need, it is not disliked. And among extravagance is if a morsel falls from his hand, he should leave it; rather he should begin with that morsel. And he should not wipe.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage details specific acts considered extravagance (israf) in the context of eating. First, eating only the soft inner part of bread (the middle) and discarding the crust is considered israf if no one will eat the discarded part. If others will eat it, it is not israf, just as choosing one loaf over another is not israf. Second, wiping hands, knives, or fingers with bread after a meal without eating the bread is israf. If one eats the bread used for wiping, it is permissible. Third, wiping hands on clothes or paper towels is not permissible if it is wasteful; but cloths for wudhu or blowing the nose are permissible. Finally, dropping a morsel and not picking it up is israf; one should pick it up and eat it. The text ends with 'he should not wipe,' likely meaning he should not wipe his hand without eating the bread.

Key Terms
وسط الخبز (wasat al-khubz): The soft inner part of bread, as opposed to the crust
حواشي الخبز (hawashi al-khubz): The edges or crust of the bread
اللقمة الساقطة (al-luqma al-saqita): A morsel that falls from the hand during eating

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is eating only the middle of the bread and leaving the crust NOT considered extravagance?
A) When the crust is thrown away
B) When someone else will eat the crust
C) When the bread is of low quality
D) When the person is full

Key Takeaway
Wasting the crust of bread is haram unless it will be consumed by another; picking up a fallen morsel is recommended.

Extravagance in Wiping Hands and Utensils with Bread and Cloths
Ruling statement on wiping actions after eating

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rulings on wiping hands with bread, on clothes, and using cloths for sweat, mucus, and wudhu, and distinguish between permissible and impermissible cases.

Core Idea
Wiping with bread is haram unless the bread is eaten; wiping hands on clothes is not allowed; cloths for sweat are only disliked if done arrogantly.

After eating, wiping hands or utensils to clean them is a practical need. However, the Hanafi school distinguishes between what is wasteful and what is permissible. This lesson focuses on the rulings for wiping with bread, on clothing, and using dedicated cloths for sweat, mucus, or wudhu. The key factor is whether the act leads to waste or arrogance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wiping actions
List the specific wiping actions discussed: wiping knife and fingers with bread, wiping hands on clothes (thiyab), on a paper towel (dastar wa raqq), and using cloths for sweat, mucus, and wudhu.

2
Classify each action
For each action, determine if it is israf (extravagance) or not based on the text.
Wiping with bread without eating it is israf; wiping on clothes is not allowed; sweat cloth is makruh if arrogant; mucus/wudhu cloth is permissible.
3
Identify conditions that change the ruling
Note the exceptions: wiping with bread is permissible if the bread is eaten afterward; using a sweat cloth is only disliked if done out of arrogance, not if needed.
4
Derive the operative ruling
Combine to formulate the mufta bihi ruling: wiping with bread without eating is haram; wiping hands on clothes is not allowed (likely makruh tahrimi); sweat cloth is makruh if arrogant, permissible if needed; other cloths are permissible.

Eating Beyond Satiety: When Excess Becomes Permissible
Ruling on eating excess with intent to vomit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating beyond satiety with the intent to vomit and apply the distinction between israf and permissible excess based on purpose.

Core Idea
Eating beyond satiety is haram if wasteful, mubah if for health—purpose defines the ruling.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a specific scenario: eating more than one's fill with the intention to vomit. It illustrates how the Hanafi school uses the concept of israf (excess) from the Quran and applies istihsan (juristic preference) to permit certain actions that would otherwise be prohibited. The ruling hinges on the presence of a valid purpose, such as medical benefit or hospitality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is eating more than one's need with the specific intent to vomit. What is the initial ruling? The text mentions that Imam Muhammad called eating beyond satiety a type of israf and declared it haram. However, the Hanafi position recognizes an exception.
Remember that the ruling depends on the purpose (qasd).
2
Locate the Primary Dalil
The primary evidence is the Quranic verse (al-A'raf 7:31): 'Eat and drink and do not be excessive.' This is the proof against israf. How does the Hanafi school interpret this verse?
The verse is qati' in transmission but zanni in meaning—it requires interpretation.
3
Understand the Hanafi Definition of Israf
Israf is not merely eating a lot; it is eating in a way that causes harm or waste. Eating beyond satiety to vomit for health reasons is not considered harmful excess because the purpose is beneficial. This is supported by the precedent of Anas ibn Malik.
This distinction is based on the principle of istihsan.
4
Note the Precedent and Its Impact
Al-Hasan al-Basri reports that Anas ibn Malik ate various foods and then vomited intentionally. The Hanafi school cites this as evidence that such an act is permissible when done for a valid reason, like health or medical treatment.
The precedent strengthens the permissibility via istihsan.
5
Derive the Final Ruling
Thus, the final Hanafi position: eating beyond satiety is haram when done out of mere excess or gluttony, but mubah (permissible) when done for a legitimate purpose such as medical benefit or hospitality. The mufta bihi is permissibility with a valid purpose.
Always consider the underlying cause (sabab) and effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية وإن أكل أكثر من حاجته ليتقاياً قال الحسن البصري: رأيت أنس بن مالك يأكل ألواناً من الطعام ويكثر ثم يتقايأ وينفع ذلك المذهب عند أصحابنا . روي عن بعض الأطباء أنه قيل له هل يجد الطبيب في كتاب الله وهو دليل تطب؟ قال : نعم قد جمع الله الطب في هذه الآية وهو قوله كلوا واشربوا ولا تسرفوا [الأعراف: ۳۱] يعني الإسراف في الأكل والشرب هو الذي منه الأمراض. وقيل : كان الرجل قليل الأكل كان أصح جسماً وأجود حفظاً وأذكى فهماً وأقل نوماً وأخف نفساً. ذكر محمد كل واحد منها من إفساد الطعام قال : ومن الإفساد الإسراف في الطعام وهو أنواع؛ فمن ذلك أن يأكل فوق الشبع فهو حرام.
And in al-Zahiriyyah: If he eats more than his need in order to vomit, al-Hasan al-Basri said: I saw Anas ibn Malik eating various types of food and eating much, then he would vomit, and that benefits the madhhab with our companions. It is narrated from some physicians that he was asked: Does the physician find in the Book of Allah a guide to medicine? He said: Yes, Allah has collected medicine in this verse: His saying: 'Eat and drink and do not be excessive' (al-A'raf 31), meaning excess in eating and drinking is what causes diseases. And it was said: A person who used to eat little had a healthier body, better memory, sharper understanding, less sleep, and lighter soul. Muhammad mentioned each one of them from the corruption of food. He said: Among the corruption is excess in food, and it is of types; among that is eating beyond satiety, and that is haram.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on eating more than one's need with the intention to vomit. Imam Muhammad mentions that some predecessors, like Anas ibn Malik, did this for medical benefit. The Hanafi school permits this for a valid purpose (like health), as it is not considered israf (excess) when done for a legitimate need. The proof is the verse prohibiting israf, but israf is defined as harmful excess. Thus, eating to vomit for health is not haram. However, general excess without purpose is haram.

Key Terms
إسراف (israf): Exceeding the limit in consumption, leading to harm or waste
تقيؤ (taqayyu'): Intentional vomiting for a purpose like health or discomfort relief

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, eating beyond satiety is haram in which case?
A) When done for medical benefit
B) When done out of excess without purpose
C) When done for guests
D) When done with intention to vomit

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits eating beyond satiety for a valid purpose like health, based on istihsan and the precedent of Anas ibn Malik.

Foods — Halal and Haram

Israf in Eating Beyond Satiety and Its Exceptions
Rulings on eating beyond need and permissible exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default ruling on eating beyond satiety and identify the exceptions where it becomes permissible.

Core Idea
Eating beyond satiety is haram unless a valid need, like hosting a guest or preparing for fasting.

This passage from al-Yanabi' clarifies a common confusion: is it always haram to eat more than one's fill? The Hanafi school teaches that the default is haram (israf), but there are specific exceptions rooted in social and devotional purposes. Let us examine these exceptions and their conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
Note that eating beyond satiety is explicitly called haram in every food.
The ruling applies to all comestibles, not just specific items.
2
Recognize the exception condition
The exception is a valid purpose (gharad sahih). The text gives two examples: avoiding embarrassment of a guest and intending to fast the next day.
The purpose must be legitimate and not mere desire.
3
Apply to variety of foods
Eating a variety of permissible foods is not israf if one is weary of a single type and aims to gain strength for obedience.
Need (hajah) can also be social, like hosting multiple groups.
4
Generalize the principle
Israf is context-dependent; actions are judged by intention and need, not solely by quantity.
This aligns with the legal maxim: al-umur bi-maqasidiha.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الينابيع : وإذا أكل الرجل فوق الشبع فهو حرام في كل مأكول. من استثنى حالة ما إذا كان له غرض صحيح في الأكل فوق الشبع فيحنئذ لا بأس به، فإن أتاه ضيف بعدما أكل قدر حاجته فليأكل لأجله حتى لا يخجل أو يريد صوم الغد فليتناول فوق الشبع. ومن الإسراف في الطعام الإسراف في المباحات والألوان فذلك منهي عنه إلا عند الحاجة بأن يمل من ناحية واحدة فليستكثر من المباحات ليستوفي من أي لون شاء فيحصل له مقدار ما يتقوى به على الطاعة، وكذلك إذا كان من قصده أن يدعو الأضياف قوماً بعد قوم إلى أن يأتوا إلى آخر الطعام فلا بأس بالاستكثار في هذه الصورة. ومن الإسراف أن يأكل وسط الخبز ويدع حواشيه ويأكل ما انتفخ من الخبز كما يفعله بعض الجهال ويزعمون أن ذلك ألذ ولكن هذا إذا كان لا يأكل غيره ما ترك من حواشيه فأما إذا كان غيره يتناول ذلك فلا بأس بذلك كما لا بأس أن يتناول رغيفاً دون رغيف. ومن الإسراف التمسح بالخبز. وفي الذخيرة ومن الإسراف مسح السكين والأصبع بالخبز عند الفراغ من الأكل من غير أن يأكل ما يتمسح فيه، فأما إذا أكل فلا بأس به. وفي التتمة سئل عمن مسح اليد على ثيابه فقال : لا يجوز وسئل عن مسح اليد بدستار ورق فقال : لا يجوز. وفي الكافي : ولا بأس بخرقة الوضوء والمخاط. وفي الجامع الصغير وتكره الخرقة التي تحمل ويمسح بها العرق إلا إذا كان شيئاً لا قيمة له وكذا الخرقة التي يمخط بها، وكذا التي بها الوضوء. وإنما يكره إذا فعل ذلك للتكبر، أما من فعل ذلك للحاجة فلا يكره، ومن الإسراف إذا سقط من يده لقمة أن يتركها بل ينبغي أن يبدأ بتلك اللقمة، وينبغي أن لا يمسح. يمسح يصب رحمهم الله السنة السنة ينتظر الإدام إذا حضر الخبز ويأخذ في الأكل قبل أن يأتي الإدام ويستحب غسل اليدين قبل الطعام فإن فيه بركة. وفي البرهانية والسنة أن يغسل الأيدي قبل الطعام وبعده. وفي واقعات الناطفي : الأدب في غسل الأيدي قبل الطعام أن يبدأ بالشبان ثم بالشيوخ، وإذا غسل لا بالمنديل لكن يترك ليجف ليكون أثر الغسل باقياً وقت الأكل والأدب في الغسل بعد الطعام أن يبدأ بالشيوخ ويمسح بالمنديل ليكون أثر الطعام زائلاً بالكلية. وفي التتمة سئل والدي عن غسل الفم للأكل هل هو سنة كغسل اليد ؟ فقال : لا وإذا غسل يده للأكل بنخالة أو غسل رأسه بذلك وأحرقها إن لم يكن فيها شيء من الدقيق وهي نخالة تعلف بها الدواب فلا بأس. وفي الذخيرة وفي نوادر هشام سألت محمداً عن غسل اليدين بالدقيق بعد الطعام هل هو مثل الغسل بالأسنان؟ فأخبرني أن أبا حنيفة وأبا يوسف لم يريا بأساً لتوارث الناس ذلك من غير نكير . وفي الخانية : ويكره للجنب رجلاً كان أو امرأة أن يأكل طعاماً وشراباً قبل غسل اليدين والفم، ولا يكره ذلك للحائض، ويستحب تطهير الفم من جميع المواضع، وينبغي أن من الآنية على يده بنفسه ولا يستعين بغيره في وضوء؛ حكي ذلك مشايخنا عن تعالى في أنه قال : هذا كالوضوء ولا يستعين بغيره في وضوء ولا يأكل طعاماً حاراً به ورد الأثر. ولا يشم الطعام فإن ذلك عمل البهائم، ولا ينفخ في الطعام والشراب، ومن أن لا يأكل الطعام من وسطه ويأكل من ابتداء الأكل ومن السنة لحس القصعة وأن يلعق أصابعه قبل أن يمسحها بالمنديل وتركه من أثر العجم والجبابرة.
And in al-Yanabi': When a man eats beyond satiety, it is haram in every food. Exception is when he has a valid purpose for eating beyond satiety; then it is not objectionable. For example, if a guest comes after he has eaten his fill, he should eat for his sake so that the guest is not embarrassed, or if he intends to fast the next day, he may eat beyond satiety. Among israf in food is excess in permissible dishes and colors; that is forbidden except when there is need: if he becomes weary of one type, he may increase from permissible things to get from any color he wishes so that he obtains strength for obedience. Similarly, if his intention is to invite guests group after group until they come at the end of the meal, there is no harm in increasing in this case. Among israf is eating the center of the bread and leaving its edges, and eating the puffed part of bread as done by some ignorant people who claim it is tastier. But this is only if no one else eats what he leaves of the edges; if someone else takes it, there is no harm, just as there is no harm in taking one loaf over another. Among israf is wiping with bread. In al-Dhakhirah: Among israf is wiping the knife and finger with bread after finishing eating without eating what is wiped off; if he eats it, no harm. In al-Tatimah: He was asked about one who wipes his hand on his clothes; he said: not permissible. He was asked about wiping the hand with a cloth of paper; he said: not permissible. In al-Kafi: No harm with a cloth for ablution and mucus. In al-Jami' al-Saghir: It is disliked to use a cloth that is carried and used to wipe sweat unless it is something of no value; likewise the cloth used for mucus, and likewise the one for ablution. It is disliked only if done out of pride; if done out of need, not disliked. Among israf: if a morsel falls from his hand, he should leave it? Rather he should begin with that morsel. And he should not wipe. They wipe, they pour, may Allah have mercy on them. The sunnah, the sunnah: he should wait for the broth when bread is present, and he should not start eating before the broth comes. It is recommended to wash hands before food, for there is blessing in it. In al-Burhaniyyah: The sunnah is to wash hands before and after food. In Waqi'at al-Natafi: The etiquette of washing hands before food is to begin with the young then the elderly; when he washes, he should not use a towel but leave them to dry so that the effect of washing remains at the time of eating. The etiquette of washing after food is to begin with the elderly and wipe with a towel so that the effect of food is completely removed. In al-Tatimah: My father was asked about washing the mouth for eating, is it sunnah like washing the hand? He said: no. And if he washes his hand for eating with bran, or washes his head with that and burns it, if there is no flour in it and it is bran fed to animals, there is no harm. In al-Dhakhirah and Nawadir Hisham: I asked Muhammad about washing hands with flour after food, is it like washing with tooth powder? He informed me that Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf did not see any harm due to people's continuous practice without criticism. In al-Khaniyyah: It is disliked for a person in major impurity, male or female, to eat or drink before washing hands and mouth, but not disliked for a menstruating woman. It is recommended to clean the mouth from all places. And it is proper that he should pour from the vessel on his own hand and not seek help from others in wudu'; our mashayikh narrated from the Exalted that He said: This is like wudu', and do not seek help from others in wudu'. And do not eat hot food, for a trace has been reported. And do not smell food, for that is the action of beasts. And do not blow into food and drink. And do not eat from the middle of the food; eat from the beginning. And it is from the sunnah to lick the bowl and to lick one's fingers before wiping them with a towel, and leaving that is from the practice of non-Arabs and tyrants.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed exposition of israf (excess/waste) in food and the sunan of eating. It covers: (1) exceptions to the prohibition of eating beyond satiety for valid purposes like guests or fasting. (2) Permissibility of variety in food when needed. (3) Prohibition of eating only the best part of bread, wasting the edges. (4) Wiping hands or utensils with bread without eating the wiped part is israf; if eaten, no harm. (5) Wiping hands on clothes or paper is not allowed. (6) Use of cloths for sweat, mucus, or ablution is disliked if done out of pride. (7) It is recommended to retrieve fallen morsels. (8) Sunnahs of eating: washing hands before and after, not wiping before drying before meal, wiping after meal; specific etiquette in washing order; not using towel before; washing mouth is not sunnah; washing with bran or flour is permissible; it is disliked for a junub to eat without washing hands/mouth but not for a menstruating woman; not to blow on food; not to smell it; not to eat from the middle; licking the bowl and fingers before wiping is sunnah. The passage emphasizes that actions are judged by intention and need, not simply by external form.

Key Terms
إسراف (israf): Excessive waste or consumption beyond need, including specific actions like wasting bread or wiping with it
غرض صحيح (gharad sahih): Valid purpose that justifies an otherwise prohibited act, such as avoiding embarrassment or preparing for worship

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, eating beyond satiety becomes permissible in which of the following situations?
A) When the food is delicious
B) When one intends to fast the next day
C) When one is tired
D) When eating with guests

Key Takeaway
Eating beyond need is israf (haram) unless there is a valid purpose such as hosting a guest or preparing for worship.

Israf in Bread: Eating Centers and Wiping
Israf in bread consumption and wiping with bread

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify acts of israf related to bread and distinguish between permissible and impermissible wiping practices.

Core Idea
Wasting bread by eating only the best part or wiping without eating is israf; wiping with bread and then eating it is not.

Bread holds a special place in Hanafi rulings on food etiquette. This passage details three forms of israf with bread: eating only the center, wiping utensils/fingers with bread without consuming the wiped portion, and discarding the edges. However, if someone else eats the edges or if one eats the wiped part, it becomes permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first israf
Eating only the center of the bread and leaving the edges is israf if no one else eats the edges.
The ignorant claim the center is tastier, but waste is condemned.
2
Note the exception to the first israf
If someone else consumes the edges, there is no harm.
Also, taking one whole loaf over another is not israf.
3
Identify the second israf
Wiping the knife or finger with bread after eating, without eating the wiped part, is israf.
The wiping itself is not the problem; the waste of bread is.
4
Distinguish permissible wiping
If the bread used for wiping is then eaten, it is not israf.
This shows the ruling is based on consumption of the bread.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الينابيع : وإذا أكل الرجل فوق الشبع فهو حرام في كل مأكول. من استثنى حالة ما إذا كان له غرض صحيح في الأكل فوق الشبع فيحنئذ لا بأس به، فإن أتاه ضيف بعدما أكل قدر حاجته فليأكل لأجله حتى لا يخجل أو يريد صوم الغد فليتناول فوق الشبع. ومن الإسراف في الطعام الإسراف في المباحات والألوان فذلك منهي عنه إلا عند الحاجة بأن يمل من ناحية واحدة فليستكثر من المباحات ليستوفي من أي لون شاء فيحصل له مقدار ما He informed me that Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf did not see any harm due to people's continuous practice without criticism. In al-Khaniyyah: It is disliked for a person in major impurity, male or female, to eat or drink before washing hands and mouth, but not disliked for a menstruating woman. It is recommended to clean the mouth from all places. And it is proper that he should pour from the vessel on his own hand and not seek help from others in wudu'; our mashayikh narrated from the Exalted that He said: This is like wudu', and do not seek help from others in wudu'. And do not eat hot food, for a trace has been reported. And do not smell food, for that is the action of beasts. And do not blow into food and drink. And do not eat from the middle of the food; eat from the beginning. And it is from the sunnah to lick the bowl and to lick one's fingers before wiping them with a towel, and leaving that is from the practice of non-Arabs and tyrants.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed exposition of israf (excess/waste) in food and the sunan of eating. It covers: (1) exceptions to the prohibition of eating beyond satiety for valid purposes like guests or fasting. (2) Permissibility of variety in food when needed. (3) Prohibition of eating only the best part of bread, wasting the edges. (4) Wiping hands or utensils with bread without eating the wiped part is israf; if eaten, no harm. (5) Wiping hands on clothes or paper is not allowed. (6) Use of cloths for sweat, mucus, or ablution is disliked if done out of pride. (7) It is recommended to retrieve fallen morsels. (8) Sunnahs of eating: washing hands before and after, not wiping before drying before meal, wiping after meal; specific etiquette in washing order; not using towel before; washing mouth is not sunnah; washing with bran or flour is permissible; it is disliked for a junub to eat without washing hands/mouth but not for a menstruating woman; not to blow on food; not to smell it; not to eat from the middle; licking the bowl and fingers before wiping is sunnah. The passage emphasizes that actions are judged by intention and need, not simply by external form.

Key Terms
إسراف (israf): Excessive waste or consumption beyond need, including specific actions like wasting bread or wiping with it
تمسح بالخبز (tamassuh bil-khubz): Wiping (hands or utensils) with bread, often practiced after meals

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Wiping a knife with bread after a meal is israf unless:
A) The bread is eaten
B) The knife is expensive
C) The bread is from the same loaf
D) It is done by a guest

Key Takeaway
Israf in bread is determined by whether the food is consumed; eating the wiped portion negates the israf.

Foods — Halal and Haram
intermediate

Rulings on Wiping Hands and Using Cloths After Eating
Wiping hands with cloths, clothes, and paper – rulings based on intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on wiping hands with cloths, clothes, and paper, and differentiate between permissible and impermissible cases based on intention.

Core Idea
Wiping hands on clothes or paper is not allowed; using a cloth for sweat is disliked if done out of pride, but permissible if needed.

After eating, how should one clean one's hands? This passage from al-Tatimah and al-Kafi clarifies that wiping directly on clothes or paper is not permissible (la yajuz). However, using a cloth for ablution or mucus is permissible. Using a cloth for sweat is disliked (makruh) if done out of pride, but not if for genuine need. Intention is key.

Guided Path
1
Identify the absolute prohibitions
Wiping the hand on one's clothes or on a paper cloth is explicitly said to be 'not permissible' (la yajuz).
This is a clear prohibition, not just dislike.
2
Recognize permissible cloth uses
Cloths used for ablution (wudu) and for mucus (mukhat) are allowed without dislike.
These are considered necessary items, not signs of pride.
3
Analyze the gradation for sweat cloths
Using a cloth to wipe sweat is disliked (makruh) if done out of pride (takabbur), but not disliked if done out of need.
The ruling changes based on intention.
4
Connect to broader adab
This shows that many actions are judged by the presence of pride; the same action can be makruh or mubah depending on intention.
This aligns with the principle 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الينابيع : وإذا أكل الرجل فوق الشبع فهو حرام في كل مأكول. من استثنى حالة ما إذا كان له غرض صحيح في الأكل فوق الشبع فيحنئذ لا بأس به، فإن أتاه ضيف بعدما أكل قدر حاجته فليأكل لأجله حتى لا يخجل أو يريد صوم الغد فليتناول فوق الشبع. ومن الإسراف في الطعام الإسراف في المباحات والألوان فذلك منهي عنه إلا عند الحاجة بأن يمل من ناحية واحدة فليستكثر من المباحات ليستوفي من أي لون شاء فيحصل له مقدار ما يتقوى به على الطاعة، وكذلك إذا كان من قصده أن يدعو الأضياف قوماً بعد قوم إلى أن يأتوا إلى آخر الطعام فلا بأس بالاستكثار في هذه الصورة. ومن الإسراف أن يأكل وسط الخبز ويدع حواشيه ويأكل ما انتفخ من الخبز كما يفعله بعض الجهال ويزعمون أن ذلك ألذ ولكن هذا إذا كان لا يأكل غيره ما ترك من حواشيه فأما إذا كان غيره يتناول ذلك فلا بأس بذلك كما لا بأس أن يتناول رغيفاً دون رغيف. ومن الإسراف التمسح بالخبز. وفي الذخيرة ومن الإسراف مسح السكين والأصبع بالخبز عند الفراغ من الأكل من غير أن يأكل ما يتمسح فيه، فأما إذا أكل فلا بأس به. وفي التتمة سئل عمن مسح اليد على ثيابه فقال : لا يجوز وسئل عن مسح اليد بدستار ورق فقال : لا يجوز. وفي الكافي : ولا بأس بخرقة الوضوء والمخاط. وفي الجامع الصغير وتكره الخرقة التي تحمل ويمسح بها العرق إلا إذا كان شيئاً لا قيمة له وكذا الخرقة التي يمخط بها، وكذا التي بها الوضوء. وإنما يكره إذا فعل ذلك للتكبر، أما من فعل ذلك للحاجة فلا يكره، ومن الإسراف إذا سقط من يده لقمة أن يتركها بل ينبغي أن يبدأ بتلك اللقمة، وينبغي أن لا يمسح. يمسح يصب رحمهم الله السنة السنة ينتظر الإدام إذا حضر الخبز ويأخذ في الأكل قبل أن يأتي الإدام ويستحب غسل اليدين قبل الطعام فإن فيه بركة. وفي البرهانية والسنة أن يغسل الأيدي قبل الطعام وبعده. وفي واقعات الناطفي : الأدب في غسل الأيدي قبل الطعام أن يبدأ بالشبان ثم بالشيوخ، وإذا غسل لا بالمنديل لكن يترك ليجف ليكون أثر الغسل باقياً وقت الأكل والأدب في الغسل بعد الطعام أن يبدأ بالشيوخ ويمسح بالمنديل ليكون أثر الطعام زائلاً بالكلية. وفي التتمة سئل والدي عن غسل الفم للأكل هل هو سنة كغسل اليد ؟ فقال : لا وإذا غسل يده للأكل بنخالة أو غسل رأسه بذلك وأحرقها إن لم يكن فيها شيء من الدقيق وهي نخالة تعلف بها الدواب فلا بأس. وفي الذخيرة وفي نوادر هشام سألت محمداً عن غسل اليدين بالدقيق بعد الطعام هل هو مثل الغسل بالأسنان؟ فأخبرني أن أبا حنيفة وأبا يوسف لم يريا بأساً لتوارث الناس ذلك من غير نكير . وفي الخانية : ويكره للجنب رجلاً كان أو امرأة أن يأكل طعاماً وشراباً قبل غسل اليدين والفم، ولا يكره ذلك للحائض، ويستحب تطهير الفم من جميع المواضع، وينبغي أن من الآنية على يده بنفسه ولا يستعين بغيره في وضوء؛ حكي ذلك مشايخنا عن تعالى في أنه قال : هذا كالوضوء ولا يستعين بغيره في وضوء ولا يأكل طعاماً حاراً به ورد الأثر. ولا يشم الطعام فإن ذلك عمل البهائم، ولا ينفخ في الطعام والشراب، ومن أن لا يأكل الطعام من وسطه ويأكل من ابتداء الأكل ومن السنة لحس القصعة وأن يلعق أصابعه قبل أن يمسحها بالمنديل وتركه من أثر العجم والجبابرة.
And in al-Yanabi': When a man eats beyond satiety, it is haram in every food. Exception is when he has a valid purpose for eating beyond satiety; then it is not objectionable. For example, if a guest comes after he has eaten his fill, he should eat for his sake so that the guest is not embarrassed, or if he intends to fast the next day, he may eat beyond satiety. Among israf in food is excess in permissible dishes and colors; that is forbidden except when there is need: if he becomes weary of one type, he may increase from permissible things to get from any color he wishes so that he obtains strength for obedience. Similarly, if his intention is to invite guests group after group until they come at the end of the meal, there is no harm in increasing in this case. Among israf is eating the center of the bread and leaving its edges, and eating the puffed part of bread as done by some ignorant people who claim it is tastier. But this is only if no one else eats what he leaves of the edges; if someone else takes it, there is no harm, just as there is no harm in taking one loaf over another. Among israf is wiping with bread. In al-Dhakhirah: Among israf is wiping the knife and finger with bread after finishing eating without eating what is wiped off; if he eats it, no harm. In al-Tatimah: He was asked about one who wipes his hand on his clothes; he said: not permissible. He was asked about wiping the hand with a cloth of paper; he said: not permissible. In al-Kafi: No harm with a cloth for ablution and mucus. In al-Jami' al-Saghir: It is disliked to use a cloth that is carried and used to wipe sweat unless it is something of no value; likewise the cloth used for mucus, and likewise the one for ablution. It is disliked only if done out of pride; if done out of need, not disliked. Among israf: if a morsel falls from his hand, he should leave it? Rather he should begin with that morsel. And he should not wipe. They wipe, they pour, may Allah have mercy on them. The sunnah, the sunnah: he should wait for the broth when bread is present, and he should not start eating before the broth comes. It is recommended to wash hands before food, for there is blessing in it. In al-Burhaniyyah: The sunnah is to wash hands before and after food. In Waqi'at al-Natafi: The etiquette of washing hands before food is to begin with the young then the elderly; when he washes, he should not use a towel but leave them to dry so that the effect of washing remains at the time of eating. The etiquette of washing after food is to begin with the elderly and wipe with a towel so that the effect of food is completely removed. In al-Tatimah: My father was asked about washing the mouth for eating, is it sunnah like washing the hand? He said: no. And if he washes his hand for eating with bran, or washes his head with that and burns it, if there is no flour in it and it is bran fed to animals, there is no harm. In al-Dhakhirah and Nawadir Hisham: I asked Muhammad about washing hands with flour after food, is it like washing with tooth powder? He informed me that Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf did not see any harm due to people's continuous practice without criticism. In al-Khaniyyah: It is disliked for a person in major impurity, male or female, to eat or drink before washing hands and mouth, but not disliked for a menstruating woman. It is recommended to clean the mouth from all places. And it is proper that he should pour from the vessel on his own hand and not seek help from others in wudu'; our mashayikh narrated from the Exalted that He said: This is like wudu', and do not seek help from others in wudu'. And do not eat hot food, for a trace has been reported. And do not smell food, for that is the action of beasts. And do not blow into food and drink. And do not eat from the middle of the food; eat from the beginning. And it is from the sunnah to lick the bowl and to lick one's fingers before wiping them with a towel, and leaving that is from the practice of non-Arabs and tyrants.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed exposition of israf (excess/waste) in food and the sunan of eating. It covers: (1) exceptions to the prohibition of eating beyond satiety for valid purposes like guests or fasting. (2) Permissibility of variety in food when needed. (3) Prohibition of eating only the best part of bread, wasting the edges. (4) Wiping hands or utensils with bread without eating the wiped part is israf; if eaten, no harm. (5) Wiping hands on clothes or paper is not allowed. (6) Use of cloths for sweat, mucus, or ablution is disliked if done out of pride. (7) It is recommended to retrieve fallen morsels. The passage emphasizes that actions are judged by intention and need, not simply by external form.

Key Terms
إسراف (israf): Excessive waste or consumption beyond need, including specific actions like wasting bread or wiping with it
خدمة خرقة (khirqah): A cloth used for wiping or cleaning

A) Need

Foods — Halal and Haram
beginner

Sunnahs of Eating: Washing Hands, Licking Bowl, and More
Recommended practices (sunan) of eating

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and apply the sunan (recommended practices) of eating according to the Hanafi school as given in this passage.

Core Idea
The Prophet's sunnah in eating includes washing hands, licking fingers, and not blowing or smelling food.

The passage concludes with a comprehensive list of sunan related to eating: from the recommended order of washing hands, to the prohibition of blowing on food, to the sunnah of licking the bowl and fingers. These actions are not obligatory but carry great reward and reflect the prophetic character.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended actions
Washing hands before and after food, licking the bowl and fingers, and beginning to eat from the edge are all sunnah.
Most are explicitly called sunnah in the text.
2
Note the etiquette of handwashing
Before eating: start with the young, let hands dry naturally. After eating: start with the elderly, wipe with a towel.
The difference in order reflects respect.
3
Identify the prohibitions
Blowing on food or drink, smelling food, and eating from the middle are not allowed.
These are considered uncivilized or beastly.
4
Recognize rulings for special states
It is disliked for a junub (major impurity) to eat without washing hands and mouth, but not for a menstruating woman.
This is a specific exception for ha'id.
5
Understand the basis
These are sunnah, not wajib. Omitting them is not sinful but loses reward.
The passage emphasizes following the Prophet's example.

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed exposition of israf (excess/waste) in food and the sunan of eating. It covers: (1) exceptions to the prohibition of eating beyond satiety for valid purposes like guests or fasting. (2) Permissibility of variety in food when needed. (3) Prohibition of eating only the best part of bread, wasting the edges. (4) Wiping hands or utensils with bread without eating the wiped part is israf; if eaten, no harm. (5) Wiping hands on clothes or paper is not allowed. (6) Use of cloths for sweat, mucus, or ablution is disliked if done out of pride. (7) It is recommended to retrieve fallen morsels. (8) Sunnahs of eating: washing hands before and after, not wiping before drying before meal, wiping after meal; specific etiquette in washing order; not using towel before; washing mouth is not sunnah; washing with bran or flour is permissible; it is disliked for a junub to eat without washing hands/mouth but not for a menstruating woman; not to blow on food; not to smell it; not to eat from the middle; licking the bowl and fingers before wiping is sunnah. The passage emphasizes that actions are judged by intention and need, not simply by external form.

Key Terms
سنة (آداب الأكل) (sunnah (adab al-akl)): Recommended practices of the Prophet in eating, such as washing hands, licking fingers, etc.
لحس القصعة (lahs al-qaṣ'ah): Licking the bowl after eating, considered a sunnah practice

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a sunnah when finishing a meal according to the passage?
A) Wiping hands on a cloth
B) Licking the bowl
C) Blowing on hot food
D) Eating from the middle

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's eating habits are sunnah, including washing hands, licking fingers, and avoiding blowing or smelling food.

Rulings on Leftover Food and Eating Habits
Compilation of rulings on eating habits and food leftovers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi ruling (sunnah, makruh, mubah, haram) for each eating-related scenario presented and explain the basis for intra-madhhab differences where applicable.

Core Idea
Hanafi fiqh provides detailed guidance on everyday eating habits, emphasizing mindfulness, necessity, and avoiding waste.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq compiles a variety of rulings on how a Muslim should handle food leftovers, eat properly, and deal with exceptional situations. From licking the bowl to feeding animals, from eating on the road to using non-edible animal urine for medicine, each act has a specific legal category. Understanding these rulings helps align daily behavior with the Shariah.

Guided Path
1
List the scenarios
Scan the passage and list each distinct scenario mentioned (e.g., licking bowl, eating fallen morsels, feeding leftovers, eating on road, carrion in necessity, Magian food, taking from declared permission, eating from oppressors, silkworms, kid raised with impure milk, urine as medicine, fallen fruit).
There are about 12 scenarios.
2
Identify the explicit ruling term
For each scenario, note the explicit word used: 'sunnah', 'makruh', 'la ba'sa', 'ja'iz', etc. For urine, note the ikhtilaf.
For example, 'licking the bowl' is from sunnah; 'eating on road' is makruh.
3
Analyze intra-madhhab differences
Focus on the urine of non-edible animals: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf prohibit; Muhammad permits. Determine which is mufta bihi.
The text states the prohibition view is more cautious.
4
Apply underlying principles
Reflect on principles like necessity (dharura) for carrion, custom (urf) for fallen fruit, and avoidance of waste (israf) for leftover bread.
These principles connect seemingly scattered rulings.

And in al-Khulasah: Among them is licking the bowl. And in al-Burhaniyyah: A man ate bread with his family and leftover breadcrumbs accumulated, and he does not desire to eat them; he may feed them to the chicken, sheep, or cat, and that is preferable. He should not throw them into the river or road unless placed for ants to eat; then it is permissible; thus some of the Salaf did. And from the sunnah is to eat what falls from the table. And from the sunnah is to begin with salt and end with salt. And in al-Sirajiyyah: Eating on the road is disliked. And eating carrion in dire necessity to the extent that wards off destruction, there is no objection. And there is no harm in the food of a Magian except the slaughtered animal. A man said: Whoever takes from my wealth is permissible, so a man took without knowing of its permissibility: it is allowed. And people should not eat from the food of oppressors; rather they should condemn them and forbid them from what they do, even if their food is lawful. Eating silkworms before the spirit is breathed into them is not objectionable. And in al-Khaniyyah: A kid goat if raised with donkey milk, Ibn al-Mubarak said: It is disliked to eat it. And a man told me from al-Hasan that he said: If a kid is raised with pig's milk, there is no harm. He said: Its meaning is if it grazes for some days, it then becomes like a jallalah. And urine of animals whose meat is not eaten, according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf, it is not permissible to use as medicine; according to Muhammad, it is permissible for medicine and other things. And it is mentioned in Uyun al-Masa'il: If a man walks through fruits in summer and wants to take from the fallen fruits under the trees, if that is in the city, he may not take unless he knows that the owner has permitted either explicitly, implicitly, or by custom. And if it is in the desert, etc. (page cut off).

Commentary
This passage covers various rulings: (1) It is sunnah to lick the bowl and eat fallen morsels. (2) Leftover breadcrumbs should be fed to animals, not thrown away; throwing in river/road is only allowed if placed for ants. (3) Eating on the road is makruh. (4) Carrion is permissible in necessity to save life. (5) Food of Magians is permissible except their slaughtered meat (because not halal slaughter). (6) If someone declares general permission, one can take even without knowing. (7) Eating from oppressors' food is discouraged to avoid supporting their tyranny. (8) Silkworms before they develop a soul are permissible to eat. (9) Kid goat raised with donkey milk is disliked (Ibn al-Mubarak); with pig milk, if it grazes after, it becomes jallalah (which has rulings). (10) Urine of non-edible animals: Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf prohibit medicinal use; Muhammad allows it. (11) Taking fallen fruit in summer: in town, need permission; in desert, presumably allowed by custom.

Key Terms
جلالة (jallalah): An animal that feeds on impure substances, requiring a period of quarantine before its meat is permissible
مخمصة (makhmashah): State of extreme hunger that threatens death

Ruling on Eating Clay: Makruh or Permissible?
Ruling on eating clay with refinements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating clay, explain when it becomes impermissible, and differentiate between problematic clay and harmless types.

Core Idea
Eating clay is makruh, not haram; it is only prohibited if it causes actual harm.

The Hanafi school classifies eating clay as makruh tanzihi, meaning it is disliked but not sinful. However, the impermissibility can escalate if health risks are involved. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the exceptions and reasoning behind the dislike, along with related etiquettes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The text opens with 'Eating clay is makruh'. Note that this is the default position in the Hanafi school.
2
Understand the condition of harm
When clay causes disease, it becomes impermissible. This is an exception to the general dislike.
Fatawa Abi al-Layth refines the ruling.
3
Evaluate the type of clay
Bahari clay is not harmful unless overused. The text distinguishes between ordinary clay and a specific type.
Al-ṭīn al-baḥārī is considered less harmful.
4
Analyze the reason for dislike
The dislike is not due to inherent prohibition but because it stirs the blood, i.e., a health effect.
5
Apply to specific cases
A woman addicted to clay may be prevented if it affects her beauty. This shows the ruling is contextual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأكل الطين مكروه وفي فتاوى أبي الليث ذكر شمس الأئمة إذا كان يخاف على نفسه من أكل الطين بأن كان يورث علة لا يباح له أكل الطير، وكذا كل شيء أكله يورث ذلك، إن كان يتناول منه قليلاً ويفعل أحياناً لا بأس به. وأكل الطين البحاري لا بأس به ما لم يسرف وكراهة أكله لا لحرمته بل لأنه يهيج الدم، والمرأة إذا اعتادت أكل الطين تمنع من ذلك إذا كان يوجب النقصان في جمالها. ولا بأس بأكل الفالوذج والأطعمة النفيسة، وعن النبي أنه أكل الرطب مع البطيخ، وأكل رضي الله عنه البطيخ مع السكر وفي التتمة : وضع الملح على القرطاس ووضعه على الخبز يجوز، وتعليق الخبز بالخوان مكروه ويكره وضع الخبز تحت القصعة، وكان الشيخ ظهر الدين المرغيناني لا يفتي بالكراهة في وضع المملحة على الخبز ولا مسح السكين بالخبز.
And eating clay is disliked. And in Fatawa Abi al-Layth, Shams al-A'imma mentioned: If he fears for himself from eating clay because it causes disease, then it is not permissible for him to eat clay. Similarly, everything that eating it causes that: if he takes from it a little and does it occasionally, there is no harm. And eating Bahari clay is not harmful as long as he does not exceed. The dislike of eating it is not due to its prohibition but because it stirs the blood. A woman who has become accustomed to eating clay is prevented from that if it causes a decrease in her beauty. And there is no harm in eating faludhaj and fine foods. And it is narrated from the Prophet that he ate fresh dates with watermelon, and Umar (RA) ate watermelon with sugar. And in al-Tatimma: Placing salt on paper and placing it on bread is permissible. Hanging bread on the dining cloth is disliked. Placing bread under the bowl is disliked. And Shaykh Zahir al-Din al-Marghinani did not give fatwa of dislike for placing the salt cellar on bread, nor for wiping the knife with bread.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement addressing the dislike of eating clay and related matters. The primary ruling: 'Eating clay is makruh.' The text then provides a refinement from Fatawa Abi al-Layth: if eating clay poses a genuine health risk (causing disease), then it becomes prohibited (la yubah) to eat it. However, if taken in small amounts occasionally, there is no harm. A distinction is made for 'Bahari clay' (a type of clay), which is not harmful unless overused; its dislike is due to medical effect (stirring blood) not inherent prohibition. A woman addicted to clay may be prevented if it harms her beauty. The text then shifts to other topics: eating fancy foods (faludhaj) is permissible; a hadith about the Prophet eating fresh dates with watermelon and Umar eating watermelon with sugar; and etiquettes about salt on paper, hanging bread, placing bread under bowl, and wiping knife on bread, with specific disagreement from al-Marghinani on some.

Key Terms
الطين البحاري (al-ṭīn al-baḥārī): A specific type of clay from Bahar (likely a region), considered less harmful.
فالوذج (fālūdhaj): A sweet dish made from starch and honey, permissible to eat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Taking Fruits from Trees Without Permission: The Hanafi Ruling
Ruling on taking fruits that remain and those that perish from trees

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for taking fruits that keep versus those that perish, and apply the preferred view regarding permissibility of eating and carrying.

Core Idea
For perishable fruits, eating is permissible unless prohibition is known; carrying is never permissible without consent.

In the summer, ripe fruits abound, and the question arises: can one take and eat fruit from a tree without the owner's explicit permission? The Hanafi school distinguishes between fruits that keep (like walnuts) and those that quickly spoil (like watermelons). This lesson unpacks the rulings, including an intra-Hanafi difference of opinion, and clarifies the preferred view (al-mukhtar).

Guided Path
1
Identify fruit categories
Read the passage and note the two categories: fruits that keep (e.g., walnuts) and fruits that perish (e.g., watermelons). Understand that the ruling differs for each.
The term 'qayz' refers to summer, the season when fruits ripen.
2
Apply the rule for fruits that keep
For fruits that keep, the passage states 'لا يسعه الأخذ إلا إذا علم الإذن' — it is not permissible to take unless one knows the owner's permission. This is the definitive ruling, confirmed in al-Ghiyathiyyah.
This implies that taking without such knowledge is haram.
3
Apply the rule for fruits that perish (ikhtilaf)
For fruits that perish, there is a difference. Al-Sadr al-Shahid's chosen view is that there is no harm in eating as long as prohibition is not known (explicitly or by custom). Al-Atabiyyah's chosen view requires knowing the owner's satisfaction. The preferred view in this commentary is the former.
This ikhtilaf is intra-Hanafi; both positions are within the school.
4
Distinguish eating from carrying
Even when eating is permissible, carrying away the fruit is not permissible without permission. The passage from Jami' al-Jawami' states 'ولا يحل حمل شيء منه' — it is not lawful to carry any of it.
Carrying implies taking substantial quantity, which causes greater loss to the owner.
5
Apply the final ruling for fruits on trees
When fruits are on the tree, the preferred view is that it is better not to take unless the owner permits or the fruit is abundant and the owner would not mind. In that case, eating is permissible but carrying is not.
This balances property rights and the custom of generosity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في القيظ فإن كان من الثمار التي تبقى مثل الجوز وغيره لا يسعه الأخذ إلا إذا علم الإذن . وفي الغياثية : هو المختار . وإن كان من الثمار التي لا تبقى اختلفوا فيه ؛ قال الصدر الشهيد : والمختار أنه لا بأس بالتناول ما لم يتبين النهي إما صريحاً أو عادة وفي العتابية : والمختار أنه لا يأكل منهما ما لم يعلم أن صاحبها رضي بذلك وإن كان ذلك في الوسواس التي يقال لها بالفارسية هراسية، فإن كان من الثمار التي لا تبقى فالمختار أنه لا بأس بالأكل ما لم يتبين النهي. وينبغي أن النهي. وفي جامع الجوامع ولا يحل حمل شيء منه، وأما إذا كان الثمار على الأشجار فالأفضل أن لا يؤخذ في موضع ما إلا أن يأذن أو يكون موضع كثير الثمار يعلم أنه لا يشق عليه أكل ذلك فيسعه الأكل ولا يسعه الحمل.
In the summer, if the fruit is of the kind that keeps (like walnuts and others), it is not permissible for him to take unless he knows permission. And in al-Ghiyathiyyah: that is the chosen. And if it is of the fruits that do not keep, they differed about it; al-Sadr al-Shahid said: the chosen is that there is no harm in taking as long as prohibition is not known, either explicitly or by custom. And in al-Atabiyyah: the chosen is that he should not eat from them until he knows that the owner is pleased with that, even if that is in the watermelon which is called in Persian 'harasiyah'. So if it is of the fruits that do not keep, the chosen is that there is no harm in eating as long as prohibition is not known. And it is appropriate that the prohibition. And in Jami' al-Jawami': it is not permissible to carry any of it. And as for when the fruits are on the trees, it is better not to take in any place unless he permits, or there is a place with abundant fruit where he knows that eating it does not burden the owner, so eating is permissible for him but carrying is not.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Karahiyyah discusses the ruling on taking fruits from trees without explicit permission. The author distinguishes between fruits that keep (like walnuts) and those that perish quickly. For fruits that keep, taking is not allowed without knowing the owner's permission. For fruits that perish, there is a difference of opinion: al-Sadr al-Shahid opts for permissibility unless prohibition is known, while al-Atabiyyah requires actual knowledge of the owner's consent. The preferred view allows eating but not carrying away. For fruits on trees, it is best not to take any unless the owner permits or the fruit is abundant and taking a little does not harm the owner.

Key Terms
القيظ (al-qayz): Summer season when fruits are ripe.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is not allowed under any circumstances.

C) It is allowed only if the owner is known to be generous.
D) It is allowed only if the fruit is abundant and the owner cannot be found.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on taking fruits balances property rights and the perishable nature of the fruit, permitting consumption of perishable fruits unless prohibition is evident, while strictly limiting carrying away.

Taking Fallen Leaves from the Road
Ruling on taking fallen leaves on a road

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on taking fallen leaves from a road, distinguish between useful and non-useful leaves, and identify the conditions that trigger liability.

Core Idea
Taking useful leaves without permission is prohibited and requires compensation; taking useless leaves is permissible.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a specific practical scenario: leaves that have fallen from a tree onto a public road. The ruling hinges on whether the leaves are of a type that people benefit from, such as mulberry leaves used for feeding silkworms. Understanding this distinction helps apply the broader Hanafi principle that ownerless property (mubah) is free to take, but property that is still of value to its owner is not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The act in question is taking leaves that have fallen from a tree onto a public road.
The act itself is not inherently prohibited; its ruling depends on the type of leaf.
2
Determine the utility of the leaf
Is the leaf of a type that people benefit from? Examples include mulberry leaves (used for silkworms) or other leaves with commercial or practical use.
The classification is based on common benefit, not potential use.
3
Apply the ruling for useful leaves
If the leaf is useful, taking it without the owner's permission is not permissible. If taken, the taker is liable (yadman) for compensation.
Liability (daman) applies even if the leaf fell naturally, because the owner retains beneficial interest.
4
Apply the ruling for non-useful leaves
If the leaf is not useful, taking it is permissible and no liability arises.
Non-useful leaves are considered ownerless (mubah) once they fall on the road.
5
Note the temporal condition
The text mentions 'ayyam al-'aylaq' – a probable season when leaves are collected. This may affect the owner's interest or custom.
The exact meaning of al-'aylaq is unclear, but it indicates that the ruling may be linked to a specific time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أوراق الأشجار إذا سقط على الطريق في أيام العيلق وأخذ إنسان شيئاً من ذلك بغير إذن صاحب الشجر ؛ فإن كان هذا ورق شجر ينتفع بورقه نحو التوت وما أشبه ذلك ليس له أن يأخذ وإن أخذ يضمن، وإن كان لا ينتفع به له أن يأخذ وإن أخذ لا يضمن.
And as for leaves of trees, if they fall on the road in the days of al-'aylaq, and a person takes something of that without the permission of the owner of the tree: if this is a leaf of a tree whose leaves are benefited from, like mulberry and the like, he may not take, and if he takes he is liable. And if it is not benefited from, he may take, and if he takes he is not liable.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This is a ruling on taking leaves that have fallen from trees onto a public road. The key distinction is whether the leaf is useful (like mulberry leaves used for feeding silkworms) or not. If useful, taking without permission is prohibited and the taker is liable for compensation. If useless (e.g., leaves that are not used for any purpose), taking is permissible and no liability arises. The time condition ('ayyam al-'aylaq) may refer to a season when leaves are typically collected.

Key Terms
العيلق (al-'aylaq): Possibly a season or time when leaves are collected; exact meaning unclear.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is permissible and he is not liable.
B) It is not permissible and he is liable for compensation.
C) It is permissible but makruh.
D) It is permissible only if he leaves something in exchange.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of taking fallen leaves depends on whether the leaves are of a type that people benefit from; if so, they remain the owner's property and taking them without permission incurs liability.
