Eating Clay: Dislike, Permission, and Prohibition
Rulings on eating clay, including medicinal and habitual use

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating clay, explain the exceptions for Bukhari clay and occasional consumption, and apply the principle that dislike can be based on harm.

Core Idea
Eating clay is disliked but not forbidden; Bukhari clay is allowed in moderation, and harm makes it haram.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq addresses the commonly asked question: Is eating clay permissible? The Hanafi school clarifies that while it is generally makruh (disliked), there are important gradations based on quantity, frequency, and potential harm. Understanding these distinctions helps avoid the misconception that clay is inherently impure or forbidden.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
Note that the text begins by stating that eating clay is makruh (disliked). This is the baseline ruling.
2
Recognize the harm exception
If eating clay causes illness (i.e., harm), it becomes impermissible. The text says: 'if he fears harm... then eating it is not permissible.'
This follows the principle that causing harm is prohibited.
3
Distinguish occasional small consumption
Taking a little clay occasionally is not problematic. This shows that the dislike is not absolute.
4
Understand the Bukhari clay exception
Clay from Bukhara is considered not harmful in small amounts, but its dislike is due to stirring the blood, not inherent prohibition.
This is a specific concession based on the practice in Bukhara.
5
Apply to women's custom
For women who have developed a habit of eating clay, they should be prevented if it harms their beauty. This ties health and social considerations.
This demonstrates that rulings can be customized based on individual circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأكل الطين مكروه وفي فتاوى أبي الليث ذكر شمس الأئمة إذا كان يخاف على نفسه من أكل الطين بأن كان يورث علة لا يباح له أكل الطير، وكذا كل شيء أكله يورث ذلك، إن كان يتناول منه قليلاً ويفعل أحياناً لا بأس به. وأكل الطين البحاري لا بأس به ما لم يسرف وكراهة أكله لا لحرمته بل لأنه يهيج الدم، والمرأة إذا اعتادت أكل الطين تمنع من ذلك إذا كان يوجب النقصان في جمالها.
And eating clay is disliked. And in Fatawa Abi al-Layth, Shams al-A'immah mentioned: if he fears for himself from eating clay, because it causes an illness, then eating bird is not permissible for him? And likewise everything whose eating causes that, if he takes a little from it and does it occasionally, there is no harm. And eating Bukhari clay is not harmful as long as he does not exceed; and the dislike of eating it is not due to its prohibition but because it stirs the blood. And a woman who becomes accustomed to eating clay is prevented from that if it causes diminishment in her beauty.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This section covers the disliked nature of eating clay. Generally, eating clay is makruh. However, if a person fears harm from eating clay (because it might cause illness), it is forbidden. But if taken in small amounts occasionally, it is permissible. The clay from Bukhara (Bukhari clay) is exceptional: it is not disliked in small quantities, but excess is discouraged because it stirs the blood. For women, if eating clay harms their beauty, they are to be prevented from it. These rulings highlight the Hanafi principle that dislikedness can be based on harm rather than inherent prohibition.

Key Terms
الطين البحاري (al-teen al-bukhari): Clay from Bukhara, considered not harmful in small amounts.

C) Because it stirs the blood.
D) Because it causes addiction.

Key Takeaway
Eating clay is generally makruh, but is permitted in small amounts from Bukhari clay, and becomes forbidden if it causes harm.

Eating Pork for Medical Treatment: A Strict Hanafi Fatwa
Question-answer ruling on compulsory pork eating for medicine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of Abu Yusuf ibn Muhammad and al-Hasan ibn Ali on eating pork for medical treatment and explain the reasoning behind their strict prohibition.

Core Idea
Eating pork is impermissible even for dire medical necessity, according to a transmitted Hanafi fatwa.

This lesson examines a specific Hanafi ruling regarding a sick person whose doctor prescribes eating pork. Two Hanafi jurists were asked whether necessity permits consuming a clearly prohibited substance. Their answer is striking: even when a trusted doctor says it is the only cure, pork remains unlawful. The passage also explores distinctions about the doctor's role and the availability of halal food, reinforcing the absolute prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Notice the setting: a sick person is told by a doctor that eating pork is necessary to recover from illness. The question is whether this necessity changes the ruling.
The scenario assumes a genuine medical need, not mere preference.
2
Extract the ruling
The jurists respond unequivocally: 'لا يحل له أكله' – it is not permissible for him to eat it. The ruling is clear and immediate.
There is no condition or exception mentioned in the first response.
3
Analyze the distinctions rejected
The questioner probes whether it matters if the doctor orders it or simply offers it, or if more halal food is available. The jurists reject all distinctions – the prohibition stands regardless.
This shows the prohibition is not contingent on external factors.
4
Understand the analogy
The jurists compare pork to wine: just as drinking wine for treatment is impermissible, so is eating pork. The analogy reinforces that necessity does not override explicit prohibitions in their view.
This analogy suggests a broader principle: what is haram for consumption remains haram even for medical treatment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التتمة سئل أبو يوسف بن محمد والحسن بن علي عن مريض قال له طبيب لا بد لك من أكل لحم الخنزير حتى يدفع عنك العلة قالا : لا يحل له أكله . وقيل هو يفرق الأمر بينهما إذا أمره بأكله أو جعله في داره فقالا : لا. قيل : ولو كان الحلال أكثر ؟ قالا : وقياس الإفتاء في شرب الخمر للتداوي أنه يجوز في لحم الخنزير.
And in al-Tatimma: Abu Yusuf ibn Muhammad and al-Hasan ibn Ali were asked about a sick person to whom a doctor said: you must eat pork to repel the illness from you. They said: it is not permissible for him to eat it. It was said: does he differentiate between the two matters if he ordered him to eat it or placed it in his house? They said: no. It was said: even if the halal is more? They said: the analogy of giving fatwa regarding drinking wine for treatment is that it is permissible in the case of pork.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This is a question addressed to two Hanafi jurists regarding the permissibility of eating pork when a doctor prescribes it as necessary medical treatment. The jurists respond unequivocally that it is not permissible. They reject any distinction between the doctor's command or merely offering the pork. Some ask about the availability of halal food: they respond with an analogy from drinking wine for treatment, suggesting that even if more halal options exist, the necessity does not justify consuming the forbidden. Note: The text mentions 'Abu Yusuf ibn Muhammad' (not the well-known Abu Yusuf) and 'Hasan ibn Ali' (likely Ziyad al-Lu'lu'i?). Their ruling is strict: pork remains unlawful even for dire medical need.

Key Terms
التداوي (al-tadawi): Medical treatment using substances.
لا يحل (la yahillu): It is not permissible; a strong prohibition indicating haram.
الخنزير (al-khinzir): Pork; the meat of the pig, which is explicitly prohibited in the Quran.
الخمر (al-khamr): Wine; intoxicating drink, used here as an analogy for prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Even when a doctor prescribes pork as necessary medical treatment, these Hanafi jurists maintain its absolute prohibition, rejecting all distinctions.

Human Tooth in Wheat – Impurity and Disposal
Ruling on wheat mixed with ground human tooth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a human tooth is ground into wheat, and identify the correct disposal method for the contaminated food.

Core Idea
Human tooth grinding into wheat renders the entire batch impure and unfit for any consumption.

Accidental mixing of impure substances with food raises important questions about purity (taharah) in Islam. In this passage, a jurist is asked about a human tooth (najis upon separation) ground into wheat. The ruling is clear: the wheat cannot be eaten by humans, nor can it be fed to animals. This principle applies to any impure substance that completely mixes with a food item, making the entire batch najis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the impurity of human body parts
Confirm that a severed human tooth is considered najis (impure) in the Hanafi school. This is because any part cut from a living human body is impure upon separation.
The impurity is not intrinsic to the tooth while attached, but becomes najis once separated.
2
Determine the effect of mixing
When the tooth is ground into wheat, its najasah (impurity) spreads to the entire wheat batch because it is thoroughly mixed. The ruling (mansus 'alayh) is that such wheat is not to be eaten.
If the impurity were a solid separate piece that could be removed, the ruling might differ.
3
Apply the ruling for human consumption
The wheat is prohibited for human consumption. This is a direct consequence of it becoming najis.
4
Apply the ruling for animal consumption
The jurist explicitly says animals should not eat it either (la ta'kuluha al-baha'im). This is because feeding najis food to animals is not permissible even if they are not Muslims; it is considered disrespectful to the animal or a form of causing harm.
Some later Hanafi scholars allow feeding najis food to animals if it is pure in itself, but here it is impure, so it is prohibited.
5
Identify proper disposal
Since neither humans nor animals can eat it, the wheat must be disposed of as najis – typically by burying it or discarding it in a way that prevents consumption.
Burying is a common method to neutralize impurity and avoid misuse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسئل عن سن الآدمي إذا طحن في الحنطة فالمنصوص عليه أن لا يؤكل وهل تدفن الحنطة أو تأكلها البهائم؟ قال : لا تأكلها البهائم
And he was asked about a human tooth if it is ground in wheat: the stipulated ruling is that it should not be eaten. And should the wheat be buried or should animals eat it? He said: animals should not eat it.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This is a question regarding a situation where a human tooth (which is impure) is accidentally ground into wheat flour. The ruling (mansus 'alayh) is that such wheat should not be consumed by humans. When asked whether the wheat should be buried or fed to animals, the jurist (likely al-Hasan ibn Ziyad or similar) says that animals should not eat it either. This indicates that the wheat becomes impure (najis) due to the tooth particles, and thus cannot be used for food for humans or animals. The appropriate disposal would be to bury it or dispose of it in a way that prevents consumption.

Key Terms
سن الآدمي (sinn al-adami): Human tooth, considered impure (najis) upon separation from the body.
المنصوص عليه (al-mansus 'alayh): The stipulated ruling; the established legal opinion on the matter.
البهام (al-baha'im): Animals (especially livestock and beasts).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It can be eaten after removing the tooth.
B) It should be fed to animals.
C) It should not be eaten by humans nor fed to animals.
D) It is permissible to eat if the tooth is small.

Key Takeaway
A human tooth ground into wheat makes the entire batch impure: it cannot be eaten by humans or fed to animals; it must be disposed of as najis.

Eating and Drinking While Reclining: The Hanafi Ruling on Posture
Ruling on the dislike of eating/drinking while reclining

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating/drinking while reclining and identify the three disliked postures mentioned in al-Atabiyyah.

Core Idea
Eating while reclining is makruh tanzihi — avoid arrogance in posture.

This brief yet important ruling from the classical text al-Atabiyyah addresses the adab (etiquette) of eating. While the act of eating is permissible, specific postures contradict the Sunnah and are therefore discouraged. The Hanafi school classifies this as makruh tanzihi, meaning it is better to avoid but not sinful. Understanding this helps us refine our daily habits toward prophetic guidance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that it is disliked (yukrahu) to eat and drink while reclining. Determine the exact hukm level: is it makruh tahrimi or tanzihi?
According to Hanafi principles, without evidence of a prohibition, it is makruh tanzihi.
2
List the postures mentioned
The text specifies three postures: (1) reclining (mutakki'an), (2) placing the left hand on the right (perhaps a posture of arrogance), and (3) leaning (mustanidan). Each is considered contrary to proper adab.
The posture of placing left hand on right might be a specific form of reclining?
3
Understand the rationale
The dislike is based on the prophetic practice of eating while sitting properly with humility. These postures are associated with pride or laziness.
No explicit dalil is given, but Hanafi scholars rely on hadith about the Prophet's way of eating.
4
Apply the ruling
When eating or drinking, avoid these postures. The ruling applies to both food and drink.
If one eats or drinks in these postures, it is valid but disliked.
5
Distinguish from other categories
Note the difference between makruh tanzihi (disliked but not sinful) and makruh tahrimi (close to haram). This is the former.
Some scholars might consider it makruh tahrimi if done out of arrogance?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي العتابية : يكره الأكل والشرب متكئاً أو واضعاً شماله على يمينه أو مستنداً.
And in al-Atabiyyah: it is disliked to eat and drink while reclining, or placing his left hand on his right, or leaning.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This brief ruling from al-Atabiyyah states that it is makruh (disliked) to eat or drink in a reclining posture. This includes leaning on one side, or placing the left hand on the right (perhaps a specific posture of arrogance?). The ruling is based on prophetic guidance that eating while sitting properly is the Sunnah. The Hanafi school considers such postures as contrary to adab (etiquette) but not sinful.

Key Terms
متكئاً (mutakki'an): Reclining, leaning on something while eating.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Prohibition of Gold and Silver Vessels
Hadith-based ruling prohibiting use of gold/silver vessels

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using gold and silver vessels and identify the hadith evidence that establishes the prohibition.

Core Idea
Using gold or silver vessels is haram for all, based on an explicit prophetic prohibition.

This passage addresses a frequently asked question: is it permissible to use vessels made of gold or silver? The author presents a clear ruling of prohibition supported by a hadith narrated by Hudhayfah. We will examine the ruling, the evidence, and the scope of the prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The acts are eating, drinking, applying oil, and perfuming using gold or silver vessels. The ruling is haram (forbidden) for both men and women.
The prohibition extends beyond eating and drinking to all forms of use.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a hadith narrated by Hudhayfah, recorded in Sahih al-Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad, where the Prophet (ﷺ) explicitly forbids drinking from gold/silver vessels and eating from their plates.
The hadith also mentions silk and brocade, but the focus here is on vessels.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibitive imperative form (لا تشربوا, لا تأكلوا) indicates absolute prohibition (haram) in Hanafi usul. The dalala is qat'i (definitive) because the command is clear.
This is a key principle: the imperative for prohibition yields haram unless there is evidence to the contrary.
4
Note the scope
The ruling applies to both men and women. The inclusion of 'applying oil' and 'perfuming' shows that the prohibition is not limited to consumption but covers any use of gold/silver vessels.
This is an expansion (probe) from the explicit text to analogous uses.
5
Consider cross-madhhab status
While the Hanafi school rules it haram, some early scholars considered it makruh. The majority (including Hanafis) follow the explicit hadith, making it mukhtalaf fih (disputed).
The strength of the evidence supports the Hanafi position of haram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( والأكل والشرب والإدهان والتطيب في إناء ذهب وفضة للرجال والنساء لما روى حذيفة أنه قال : سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول «لا تلبسوا الحرير ولا الديباج ولا تشربوا في آنية الذهب والفضة ولا تأكلوا في صحافها وإنها لهم في الدنيا ولكم في الآخرة» (۱) رواه البخاري ومسلم وأحمد.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And eating, drinking, applying oil, and perfuming in a vessel of gold and silver is [prohibited] for men and women, because Hudhayfah narrated that he said: I heard the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) say: 'Do not wear silk and brocade, and do not drink from vessels of gold and silver, and do not eat from their plates, for they are for them in this world and for you in the Hereafter.' ) Narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This is a clear ruling from the author (likely al-Marghinani or the commentator) that using gold and silver vessels for eating, drinking, applying oil, or perfuming is prohibited for both men and women. The proof is the authentic hadith narrated by Hudhayfah, in which the Prophet (ﷺ) explicitly forbids drinking from gold and silver vessels and eating from their plates. The hadith explains that these vessels are for the disbelievers in this world and for Muslims in the Hereafter (i.e., Paradise). This ruling is agreed upon in the Hanafi school and is based on this explicit prohibition. The mention of 'oil' and 'perfume' extends the prohibition to all uses, not just eating and drinking.

Key Terms
الإدهان (al-idhan): Applying oil (e.g., to hair or body).
آنية الذهب والفضة (aniyat al-dhahab wa al-fidda): Vessels made of gold or silver.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Eating, drinking, applying oil, or perfuming using gold or silver vessels is haram for both men and women, based on an authentic prophetic command.

Eating Forbidden Foods for Medical Necessity
Fatwa rulings on eating forbidden foods for medical reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on consuming pork, snake, or hedgehog for medical reasons and identify the exception for mouse-eaten wheat due to necessity.

Core Idea
Pork and snakes are never permissible even with a doctor's order; mouse-eaten wheat is permitted only under dire necessity.

This lesson addresses a common scenario where a doctor prescribes eating something normally forbidden. The Hanafi scholars provide clear boundaries: medical necessity does not override the general prohibition of pork and other impure animals. However, they recognize a narrow exception for wheat contaminated by mice, permissible only when no other food is available.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibition
The passage begins by reaffirming that pork is haram (impermissible) for consumption. The question is whether a doctor's prescription can override this.
2
Analyze the response to medical necessity
Abu Yusuf and al-Hasan ibn Ali rule that even if a doctor says pork is necessary to repel an illness, it remains impermissible. They reject any distinction between ordering to eat or placing it in the house.
They analogize to wine for treatment, which they permit, but they do not extend this to pork.
3
Examine the ruling on snake and hedgehog
Al-Hasan ibn Ali is asked about eating snake or hedgehog, or medicine containing snake, if a skilled doctor indicates it repels illness. He rules it is not permissible.
These animals are considered impure (najis) and not permissible even for treatment.
4
Understand the exception for mouse-eaten wheat
When a mouse eats some wheat, the question is whether the remaining wheat can be consumed. The ruling states it is permitted only due to necessity (darurah).
This is a specific exception: the wheat is not impure, but the mouse's saliva may be considered impure; necessity allows it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن نعم والأصبع، ومن المشايخ من أفتى بالكراهة. وفي التتمة سئل أبو يوسف بن محمد والحسن بن علي عن مريض قال له طبيب لا بد لك من أكل لحم الخنزير حتى يدفع عنك العلة قالا : لا يحل له أكله . وقيل هو يفرق الأمر بينهما إذا أمره بأكله أو جعله في داره فقالا : لا. قيل : ولو كان الحلال أكثر ؟ قالا : وقياس الإفتاء في شرب الخمر للتداوي أنه يجوز في لحم الخنزير. وسئل الحسن بن علي عن أكل الحية والقنفذ أو أكل الدواء الذي فيه الحية أحمد إذا أشار الطبيب الحاذق بأنه يدفع العلة هل يحل أكله؟ قال: لا. وسئل علي بن خبز الخبز على نوعين نوع للجواري ونوع لنفسه ويأكل ما يجعل لنفسه هل يأثم؟ قال : يكره له ذلك. وسئل عن سؤر الهرة إذا عجن فيه الدقيق وخبز هل يكره أكله؟ قال : لا . وسئل عن الخبز إذا عجن بالحليب قال : لا يكره ولا بأس به. وعن قطع اللحم بالسكين قال : لا بأس به. وسئل عن عرق الآدمي ونخامته ودمعه إذا وقع في المرقة أو في الماء هل يأكل المرقة ويشرب الماء؟ قال نعم ما لم يغلب ويصير مستقذراً طبعاً. وسئل عن سن الآدمي إذا طحن في الحنطة فالمنصوص عليه أن لا يؤكل وهل تدفن الحنطة أو تأكلها البهائم؟ قال : لا تأكلها البهائم وسئل عن الفأرة تأكل الحنطة هل يجوز أكلها؟ قال : لأجل الضرورة. وسئل أبو الفضل عن إشعال التنور بأخثاء البقر هل يجوز إذا خبز بها الخبز ؟ قال : يجوز أكل ذلك الخبز. وسئل أبو حامد عن شعل التنور بأرواث الحمر هل يخبز بها؟ قال : يكره ولو رش عليه ماء بطلت الكراهة وعليه عرف أهل العراق ورماده طاهر. وفي العتابية : يكره الأكل والشرب متكئاً أو واضعاً شماله على يمينه أو مستنداً. ولا يسقي أباه الكافر خمراً ولا يناوله القدح ويأخذه منه ولا يذهب به إلى البيعة ويرده منها، ويوقد تحت قدره إذا لم يكن فيه ميتة. وفي النوازل قال محمد بن مقاتل: البطنة بطنتان: أحدهما أن يتعمد الرجل السمن وعظم البطن فإن هذا مكروه، فأما بطناً عظيماً وكان ذلك خلقاً من غير أن يتعمد السمن فلا شيء عليه. قال الفقيه : التأويل في الخبز الذي ورد عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ( إن الله يبغض الحبر السمين معناه إذا تعمد السمن، أما إذا خلقه الله سميناً فهو غير داخل في الخبز اهـ وفي السراجية : ويكره أن يلبس الرجل ثوباً فيه كتابة بذهب وفضة روي أنه قول أبي يوسف، وعلى قياس قول الإمام لا يكره فلا بأس بلبسه اهـ.
From 'yes' and the finger. Some scholars gave a fatwa of dislike. In al-Tatimma: Abu Yusuf ibn Muhammad and al-Hasan ibn Ali were asked about a sick person whose doctor said: 'You must eat pork to repel the illness.' They said: It is not permissible for him to eat it. It was said: Is there a difference between the two cases if the doctor ordered him to eat it or put it in his house? They said: No. It was said: Even if halal food is more? They said: By analogy with the fatwa on drinking wine for treatment, that it is permissible for pork. Al-Hasan ibn Ali was asked about eating snake and hedgehog, or eating a medicine containing snake, if a skilled doctor indicates it repels the illness, is it permissible to eat it? He said: No. Ali ibn [unknown] was asked about bread made in two types: one for slave girls and one for himself, and he eats what is made for himself, is he sinful? He said: That is disliked for him. He was asked about the leftover of a cat if flour is kneaded with it and bread is made, is eating it disliked? He said: No. He was asked about bread kneaded with milk: He said: It is not disliked and there is no harm. About cutting meat with a knife: He said: There is no harm. He was asked about human sweat, phlegm, and tears if they fall into soup or water, should he eat the soup and drink the water? He said: Yes, as long as they do not dominate and become naturally repulsive. He was asked about a human tooth if ground into wheat: the stated ruling is that it is not eaten, and should the wheat be buried or eaten by animals? He said: Animals should not eat it. He was asked about a mouse that eats wheat, is it permissible to eat it? He said: Due to necessity. Abu al-Fadl was asked about lighting the oven with cow dung, is it permissible if bread is baked with it? He said: Eating that bread is permissible. Abu Hamid was asked about lighting the oven with donkey dung, should bread be baked with it? He said: It is disliked, but if water is sprinkled on it, the dislike is removed, and that is the custom of the people of Iraq, and its ash is pure. In al-Atabiyya: It is disliked to eat and drink while reclining, or placing the left hand on the right, or leaning. And one should not give wine to his disbelieving father, nor hand him the cup and take it from him, nor go with him to the church and bring him back, but he may light fire under his pot if there is no carrion in it. In al-Nawazil, Muhammad ibn Muqatil said: Potbelly is of two types: one is that a man intentionally seeks fatness and a large belly – this is disliked. As for a large belly that is natural without intentional fatness, there is nothing on him. Al-Faqih said: The interpretation of the hadith 'Indeed Allah hates the fat scholar' means if he intentionally seeks fatness, but if Allah created him fat, he is not included in the hadith. In al-Sirajiyya: It is disliked for a man to wear a garment having writing in gold and silver; it is reported that this is the view of Abu Yusuf, and by analogy with the Imam's view it is not disliked, so there is no harm in wearing it.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage contains a collection of rulings (fatawa) from various Hanafi scholars on everyday matters. It begins with a question about eating pork for medical necessity: even if a doctor prescribes it, the scholars say it remains impermissible. However, they note a ruling on drinking wine for treatment that permits it; they apply similar reasoning to pork. Next, eating snake or hedgehog for medicine is not allowed even with a doctor's advice. Then issues of bread preparation: if a man makes different bread for himself and his slaves, eating only his own is disliked. The leftover water of a cat used in dough is not disliked. Bread with milk is fine. Cutting meat with a knife is fine. Human sweat, phlegm, tears falling into food/drink: permissible unless they overwhelm and become repulsive. A human tooth ground into wheat: the wheat should not be eaten nor fed to animals. Wheat eaten by a mouse: permitted only due to necessity. Oven fuel from cow dung: bread baked is permissible; from donkey dung: disliked, but washing removes dislike. Reclining while eating/drinking is disliked, as is placing left hand on right or leaning. One should not serve wine to a non-Muslim father, nor assist him in going to church, but may light fire under his cooking pot if no carrion. Intentional potbelly is disliked; natural obesity is not. The hadith 'Allah hates the fat scholar' refers to intentional seeking of fatness. Finally, wearing a garment with gold/silver writing: according to Abu Yusuf it is disliked, but by qiyas with Abu Hanifa's view it is not disliked.

Key Terms
ضرورة (darurah): Dire necessity that permits otherwise prohibited acts, such as eating carrion to avoid death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi scholars in this passage, what is the ruling on eating snake meat if a doctor says it is necessary for recovery?
A) Permissible due to necessity
B) Permissible only if no other treatment exists
C) Haram even if prescribed by a doctor
D) Makruh, but not haram

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh maintains that inherently impure foods like pork and snake remain forbidden even for medical treatment, except in cases of direct life-threatening necessity narrowly defined.

Foods — Halal and Haram
beginner
10 min
Rulings on Food Preparation and Utensils
Fatwa rulings on common food preparation practices and fuel usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on using cat su'ur in dough, milk in bread, cutting meat with a knife, human fluids in food, and fuel from animal dung.

Core Idea
Most everyday practices in food preparation are permissible unless they involve impurity or cause repulsion; doubtful cases are resolved by custom and necessity.

This lesson covers a variety of everyday scenarios: using leftover water of a cat, kneading bread with milk, cutting meat with a knife, and the effect of human sweat/phlegm/tears in food. It also addresses fuel from cow and donkey dung for baking. The Hanafi rulings are generally lenient, declaring most practices permissible, with some dislikes that can be removed by simple actions.

Guided Path
1
Classify the cat's su'ur (leftover water)
The passage states that using cat's leftover water in dough is not disliked. Recognize that cat's su'ur is considered pure in the Hanafi school.
This is based on a hadith that cats are not impure.
2
Examine bread kneaded with milk
The ruling is that bread made with milk is not disliked and there is no harm (la ba'sa bihi).
Milk is pure, so no issue.
3
Understand cutting meat with a knife
Cutting meat with a knife is permissible (la ba'sa bihi). This addresses a possible concern about the knife contacting meat.
No restriction mentioned.
4
Analyze human fluids in food
Sweat, phlegm, and tears of a human falling into soup or water are excused as long as they do not dominate and become naturally repulsive (mustaqdhar).
Hanafis consider human fluids pure but repulsive if quantity overwhelms.
5
Review rulings on fuel for ovens
Cow dung fuel: bread baked is permissible. Donkey dung fuel: disliked, but dislike is removed if water is sprinkled on it. The ash is pure.
Custom of Iraq is cited; donkey dung is considered impure by some but washing lifts the dislike.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن نعم والأصبع، ومن المشايخ من أفتى بالكراهة. وفي التتمة سئل أبو يوسف بن محمد والحسن بن علي عن مريض قال له طبيب لا بد لك من أكل لحم الخنزير حتى يدفع عنك العلة قالا : لا يحل له أكله . وقيل هو يفرق الأمر بينهما إذا أمره بأكله أو جعله في داره فقالا : لا. قيل : ولو كان الحلال أكثر ؟ قالا : وقياس الإفتاء في شرب الخمر للتداوي أنه يجوز في لحم الخنزير. وسئل الحسن بن علي عن أكل الحية والقنفذ أو أكل الدواء الذي فيه الحية أحمد إذا أشار الطبيب الحاذق بأنه يدفع العلة هل يحل أكله؟ قال: لا. وسئل علي بن خبز الخبز على نوعين نوع للجواري ونوع لنفسه ويأكل ما يجعل لنفسه هل يأثم؟ قال : يكره له ذلك. وسئل عن سؤر الهرة إذا عجن فيه الدقيق وخبز هل يكره أكله؟ قال : لا . وسئل عن الخبز إذا عجن بالحليب قال : لا يكره ولا بأس به. وعن قطع اللحم بالسكين قال : لا بأس به. وسئل عن عرق الآدمي ونخامته ودمعه إذا وقع في المرقة أو في الماء هل يأكل المرقة ويشرب الماء؟ قال نعم ما لم يغلب ويصير مستقذراً طبعاً. وسئل عن سن الآدمي إذا طحن في الحنطة فالمنصوص عليه أن لا يؤكل وهل تدفن الحنطة أو تأكلها البهائم؟ قال : لا تأكلها البهائم وسئل عن الفأرة تأكل الحنطة هل يجوز أكلها؟ قال : لأجل الضرورة. وسئل أبو الفضل عن إشعال التنور بأخثاء البقر هل يجوز إذا خبز بها الخبز ؟ قال : يجوز أكل ذلك الخبز. وسئل أبو حامد عن شعل التنور بأرواث الحمر هل يخبز بها؟ قال : يكره ولو رش عليه ماء بطلت الكراهة وعليه عرف أهل العراق ورماده طاهر. وفي العتابية : يكره الأكل والشرب متكئاً أو واضعاً شماله على يمينه أو مستنداً. ولا يسقي أباه الكافر خمراً ولا يناوله القدح ويأخذه منه ولا يذهب به إلى البيعة ويرده منها، ويوقد تحت قدره إذا لم يكن فيه ميتة. وفي النوازل قال محمد بن مقاتل: البطنة بطنتان: أحدهما أن يتعمد الرجل السمن وعظم البطن فإن هذا مكروه، فأما بطناً عظيماً وكان ذلك خلقاً من غير أن يتعمد السمن فلا شيء عليه. قال الفقيه : التأويل في الخبز الذي ورد عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ( إن الله يبغض الحبر السمين معناه إذا تعمد السمن، أما إذا خلقه الله سميناً فهو غير داخل في الخبز اهـ وفي السراجية : ويكره أن يلبس الرجل ثوباً فيه كتابة بذهب وفضة روي أنه قول أبي يوسف، وعلى قياس قول الإمام لا يكره فلا بأس بلبسه اهـ.
From 'yes' and the finger. Some scholars gave a fatwa of dislike. In al-Tatimma: Abu Yusuf ibn Muhammad and al-Hasan ibn Ali were asked about a sick person whose doctor said: 'You must eat pork to repel the illness.' They said: It is not permissible for him to eat it. It was said: Is there a difference between the two cases if the doctor ordered him to eat it or put it in his house? They said: No. It was said: Even if halal food is more? They said: By analogy with the fatwa on drinking wine for treatment, that it is permissible for pork. Al-Hasan ibn Ali was asked about eating snake and hedgehog, or eating a medicine containing snake, if a skilled doctor indicates it repels the illness, is it permissible to eat it? He said: No. Ali ibn [unknown] was asked about bread made in two types: one for slave girls and one for himself, and he eats what is made for himself, is he sinful? He said: That is disliked for him. He was asked about the leftover of a cat if flour is kneaded with it and bread is made, is eating it disliked? He said: No. He was asked about bread kneaded with milk: He said: It is not disliked and there is no harm. About cutting meat with a knife: He said: There is no harm. He was asked about human sweat, phlegm, and tears if they fall into soup or water, should he eat the soup and drink the water? He said: Yes, as long as they do not dominate and become naturally repulsive. He was asked about a human tooth if ground into wheat: the stated ruling is that it is not eaten, and should the wheat be buried or eaten by animals? He said: Animals should not eat it. He was asked about a mouse that eats wheat, is it permissible to eat it? He said: Due to necessity. Abu al-Fadl was asked about lighting the oven with cow dung, is it permissible if bread is baked with it? He said: Eating that bread is permissible. Abu Hamid was asked about lighting the oven with donkey dung, should bread be baked with it? He said: It is disliked, but if water is sprinkled on it, the dislike is removed, and that is the custom of the people of Iraq, and its ash is pure. In al-Atabiyya: It is disliked to eat and drink while reclining, or placing the left hand on the right, or leaning. And one should not give wine to his disbelieving father, nor hand him the cup and take it from him, nor go with him to the church and bring him back, but he may light fire under his pot if there is no carrion in it. In al-Nawazil, Muhammad ibn Muqatil said: Potbelly is of two types: one is that a man intentionally seeks fatness and a large belly – this is disliked. As for a large belly that is natural without intentional fatness, there is nothing on him. Al-Faqih said: The interpretation of the hadith 'Indeed Allah hates the fat scholar' means if he intentionally seeks fatness, but if Allah created him fat, he is not included in the hadith. In al-Sirajiyya: It is disliked for a man to wear a garment having writing in gold and silver; it is reported that this is the view of Abu Yusuf, and by analogy with the Imam's view it is not disliked, so there is no harm in wearing it.

Key Terms
سؤر الهرة (su'ur al-hirah): The leftover water after a cat drinks.
مستقذر (mustaqdhar): Something that is naturally repulsive or disgusting to the average person.

Key Takeaway

Everyday Etiquette and Social Interactions
Fatwa rulings on etiquette, social conduct, and personal grooming.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi rulings on eating posture, serving wine to a non-Muslim father, intentional obesity, and wearing garments with gold or silver writing.

Core Idea
Etiquette is important: reclining while eating is disliked, serving wine to a non-Muslim father is prohibited, intentional obesity is disliked, and gold/silver writing on clothes is subject to scholarly difference.

This lesson covers four distinct but everyday matters: proper eating posture, interacting with a non-Muslim parent regarding wine, the ruling on deliberately gaining a potbelly, and wearing clothes with gold or silver inscriptions. These show how Hanafi fiqh blends legal rulings with moral guidance and social norms.

Guided Path
1
Analyze eating and drinking etiquette
The passage states it is disliked to eat or drink while reclining, placing the left hand on the right, or leaning. This is a matter of adab (etiquette) derived from the Sunnah.
These postures are seen as arrogant or mimicking non-Muslim customs.
2
Understand interaction with a non-Muslim father regarding wine
One should not give wine to his disbelieving father, nor hand him the cup nor take it from him. However, one may light a fire under his cooking pot if there is no carrion (dead animal) in it.
This balances filial duty with prohibition of facilitating sin.
3
Examine the ruling on intentional potbelly (batana)
Muhammad ibn Muqatil differentiates between intentionally seeking a large belly (disliked) and natural obesity (not disliked). The hadith about Allah hating the fat scholar refers to intentional fatness.
This shows the importance of intention (niyyah) in determining ethical rulings.
4
Review the difference on gold/silver writing on clothes
According to Abu Yusuf, wearing a garment with gold or silver writing is disliked. By analogy with Abu Hanifa's view (qiyas), it is not disliked, and there is no harm. The passage indicates a difference within the school.
The mufta bihi may be the more lenient view.

إمتلاء (imtila'): Reclining posture while eating.

Key Takeaway
